"},{"id":40201,"link":"https:\/\/www.nba.com\/news\/doug-moe-2018-chuck-daily-lifetime-achievement-award","date":"2018-06-03","title":"Doug Moe receives 2018 Chuck Daly Lifetime Achievement Award","content":"OAKLAND \u2014 It\u2019s tricky giving Doug Moe the credit he\u2019s due as one of the godfathers of today\u2019s fast-paced, big-scoring, offensive-minded style of pro basketball. There\u2019s no book to prove it. No Tex Winter textbook to dust off and revive the way Phil Jackson did with the triangle offense. No newer, shinier \u201cSeven Seconds Or Less\u201d account of Mike D\u2019Antoni\u2019s high-revving system crafted in Phoenix and practiced these days in Houston. There\u2019s no book dedicated to Moe\u2019s preferred style of play for the San Antonio Spurs and the Denver Nuggets in the 1970s and \u201880s. And even if there was, it would be a richly bound volume of blank pages. \u201cNobody knew what the hell we were doing,\u201d Moe said back then. \u201cOther coaches were diagramming our plays on the blackboard, and we weren\u2019t running any.\u201d The man who turned nothing into a real cool hand and usually had the last laugh on Sunday was named the 2018 recipient of the Chuck Daly Lifetime Achievement Award, presented by the National Basketball Coaches Association. Moe, 79, was scheduled to be honored at Oracle Arena about an hour before tipoff of Game 2 of The NBA Finals. A former ABA star who broke into coaching as an assistant to Hall of Famer Larry Brown, Moe compiled a 628-529 (.543) record in four seasons with the Spurs (1976-1980), 10 with the Nuggets (1980-1990) and one with the 76ers (1992-93). He did it with a system built on ball movement, screens, cuts and a commitment to players holding the ball for no longer than two seconds. It relied more on instinct, reflexes and athletic ability in reacting to whatever look the defense gave, rather than on some high-minded philosophy of Xs & Os. Among the Hall of Fame players who thrived in Moe\u2019s style: George Gervin, David Thompson and Alex English.  \u201cIn the 1970s and 80s, Doug Moe established a pace-and-space game that was decades ahead of its time,\u201d said Dallas Mavericks coach Rick Carlisle, also president of the NBCA who made the presentation. \u201cCongratulations to a true visionary on this special recognition of innovation and accomplishment.\u201d Moe called his offense \u201cthe passing game\u201d only because that sounded better \u2013 at least to the owners signing his paycheck \u2013 than describing it as the freelancing, read-and-react style it was. He playbook, as he described the style Sunday, was about this thick: \u201cGo out there, do what you want. Have a good time. Just make sure we win the game.\u201d \u201cPeople think we just roll the ball out there and play, and to some extent that\u2019s true,\u201d Moe told the Chicago Tribune in January 1988 , midway through his NBA Coach of the Year award season. Denver went 54-28 in 1987-88 and reached the playoffs, marking the 10th of 12 appearances there for Moe-coached teams. \u201cBut it takes discipline to play in our system,\u201d Moe said. \u201cBasically what we`re trying to do is keep teams off balance, not let them get set. We`re pretty good defensively. We`re not a great shooting team. But we get up more shots than our opponents, which allows us to shoot a little poorer. Our whole philosophy is to attack.\u201d In the decade that Moe coached the Nuggets, they led the NBA in scoring six times, finished second twice and ranked third once. The 1981-82 team averaged 126.5 points per game, which remains the highest single-season scoring average in league history. Of course, they gave up 126 points per game, also a record. Moe was Denver\u2019s coach on Dec. 13 1983 when the Nuggets scored 184 points \u2013 and lost to Detroit, which scored 186 in the triple-overtime affair. The Pistons were coached that day by Daly. Moe also grasped early what so many basketball experts have come to only recently: the concept of pace and how it related to the game\u2019s numbers. \u201cMost of my career, we\u2019ve been first in offense and last in defense,\u201d Moe told Sports Illustrated during his time in Denver . \u201cBut what people don\u2019t realize is that total scores have nothing to do with defense or offense, just the pace of the game. It\u2019s the dumbest statistic ever, totally wacko, and yet everyone uses the total scores as an indication of the kind of defense you play. \u201cI may not be the smartest guy in the world, but as long as people go by that stat, I know there\u2019s someone out there dumber than I am.\u201d The above quotes provide a glimpse of Moe\u2019s other distinguishing characteristic as an NBA coach. He was in it as much for the fun as for the competition or the cash. While any book on his offense might be nothing more than blank pages, an anthology of his wit and street-variety wisdom might require a second volume. \u201cDoug was the original free spirit,\u201d said longtime NBA writer Mike Monroe, who spent most of his career in Denver and San Antonio. \u201cHe was the ringleader of all kinds of stuff.\u201d Moe was notorious for his short and casual practices. He irritated the more button-down, whistle-toting types among his coaching brethren. He could get furious on the sideline while a game hung in the balance \u2014 one broadcaster called him \u201cbilingual,\u201d for Moe\u2019s mastery of both English and profanity \u2014 and in 1979 former NBA commissioner Larry O\u2019Brien once fined him $5,000 for suggesting in a postgame remark that the night\u2019s referees \u201cbe set before a firing squad.\u201d After a sufficient cooling-off period, though, that and every other game was over. Moe had an ability to move on almost blithely, like few in his profession. \u201cWhen Doug gets on you, his voice cuts through to your soul,\u201d Kiki Vandeweghe, currently NBA Executive Vice President, Basketall Operations, once said while playing for Moe in Denver. \u201cIf you play poorly, he\u2019ll yell and scream and hate you during the game. But after the game you can still be the best of friends.\u201d Monroe talked about Moe\u2019s penchant for needling anyone and everyone, particularly those of whom he was most fond. \u2018\u201cDips\u2019 is what he called you,\u201d Monroe said. \u201cAnd he\u2019s the head dip[bleep]. He has his \u2018Dip lunches,\u2019 where everybody was insulting and busting on each other. The only rule: no basketball talk. And Doug would always pick up the tab.\u201d A native of Brooklyn born in 1938, Moe was recruited to the University of North Carolina. While home one summer, he was offered a bribe to participate in a point-shaving scheme. He declined it but traveled out of friendship with another player to meet with the gambler. When Moe accepted $75 offered for his traveling expenses to and from the meeting, he put himself in the crosshairs of a legal crackdown on collegiate basketball at that time. Drafted by the Chicago Packers in 1961, Moe \u2014 though cleared \u2014 wound up blackballed in the NBA. He played professionally for a couple of seasons in Italy, then came back to the States when the American Basketball Association opened for business in 1967. He was a three-time All-Star in that upstart league, playing for four teams in five seasons, before knee problems ended his playing career. That\u2019s when his pal Larry Brown sought him out as an assistant coach, a role Moe, without any prodding, would have been happy to hold for life. Ambition is not high on his list of life\u2019s priorities. \u201cMy big advantage over other people,\u201d Moe said when he was 50, \u201cis that I\u2019ve always been less mature than my age. Even though my body is old and I can barely walk, mentally I\u2019m still 12.\u201d For instance, he has always called his wife \u201cBig Jane\u201d for \u201cno particular reason\u201d despite her normal stature. Despite his career-long quest for fun and flippant approach to many topics, Moe does sound proud of his place in crafting an offensive approach more embraced than ever in the NBA. He was thrilled back in the \u201870s when the league adopted the ABA\u2019s 3-point shot, the cornerstone of today\u2019s game. The question is inevitable: If the 3-pointer were more widely embraced in the 1980s, would Moe\u2019s have exploited it? Keep in mind, those 1981-82 Nuggets that averaged 126.5 point took a total of 149 3-pointers that season, making just 40. The average team in 2017-18 shot 2,378 3-pointers and made 860 \u2013 yet averaged 106.3 points. \u201cI don\u2019t think so,\u201d Moe said Sunday at the Daly presentation. \u201c[Our players] could shoot a 3-pointer anytime they wanted. It was up to the players. The problem is, you\u2019ve got to be able to [make] \u2018em. \u201cWe only had one or two guys who ever shot threes. Then we had great scorers in [Alex] English, Kiki Vandeweghe and Dan Issel. So at that time, we didn\u2019t need quite as many threes. But they were free to do whatever they wanted. \u201cIt was a fun way to play and, after you play it a while, the players don\u2019t want to run plays. If things started going bad, I\u2019d threaten to run plays and they\u2019d always start playing better.\u201d The NBCA Daly award, which honors the Naismith Basketball Hall of Famer and original \u201cDream Team\u201d coach, is chosen by the Award Selection Committee. Its members include: Bernie Bickerstaff, Billy Cunningham, Joe Dumars, Phil Jackson, Gregg Popovich, Pat Riley, Donnie Walsh and Lenny Wilkens. Previous recipients are Al Attles and Hubie Brown (2017), K.C. Jones and Jerry Sloan (2016), Dick Motta (2015), Bickerstaff (2014), Bill Fitch (2013), Pat Riley (2012), Wilkens (2011), Tex Winter and Jack Ramsay (2010) and Tommy Heinsohn (2009). One is Andre Iguodala (2015), who has a bone bruise, didn\u2019t suit up for Game 1 and perhaps won\u2019t be healthy for Game 2 , (8 ET, on ABC) either. The second is LeBron James (2016), who went nuclear in the opener with 51 points and his searing post-season run has the Warriors on high alert. And the third is Kevin Durant (2017) who right now is flip-flopping between Iguodala and LeBron: Half-missing, half-dangerous. If not for the follies of the referees, George Hill and especially JR Smith in Game 1, the player on the griddle right now would be Durant, who remained drifting in and out of a mysterious shooting funk and was bailed out by an overturned call, a missed free throw and a forgetful Cavaliers guard. Taking it a step further, Steph Curry took the mic from Durant and is now the Warriors\u2019 lead singer at least until further notice, or when Durant remembers what the summer of 2017 was like for him. He\u2019s not the Durant of last year\u2019s Finals, not yet anyway, and you wonder if this could cost the Warriors a game or two, or even the series if Klay Thompson\u2019s bum ankle becomes a problem and LeBron stays locked in his trance of a historic level. \u201cHe hasn\u2019t probably been as consistent as he was last year,\u201d said Warriors coach Steve Kerr, \u201cbut neither have we. I would say that about every one of our guys. It\u2019s been a little different vibe, but that\u2019s OK. Every trip is a little different. We\u2019re where we want to be and we\u2019re all very confident that we\u2019re going to get better from here.\u201d The opening game of this series reflected Durant\u2019s entire postseason. Sharp one moment and flawed the next, these lapses are alarming only because it\u2019s quite a contrast from the Durant who was the best player in a series that included LeBron in Warriors-Cavaliers III. And also because James is on such an elevated level right now, ripping through defenses and strutting while Durant often struggles. The contrast between the two is wider than expected. Durant\u2019s fingerprints were all over the final crazy seconds of Game 1, where he could\u2019ve easily been the goat. First: After apparently losing confidence in his jumper after missing seven of eight 3-point shots, Durant forced his way to the rim and drew contact with LeBron, who provided late help. The initial call of an offensive foul was overturned only because another referee mistakenly thought LeBron was in the restricted area, which gave the refs a chance to use instant replay and call LeBron for blocking instead. Second: After Hill missed a free throw that would\u2019ve given the Cavs a one-point lead with 4.7 seconds left, Durant forgot to box out Smith, who snuck in for the rebound. Warriors coach Steve Kerr at that moment was incredulous, but unless you\u2019ve been living in a cave, you\u2019re aware of what Smith did right after that , taking Durant off the hook. Also: With Iguodala sidelined, Durant didn\u2019t offer much resistance against LeBron, who shot a high percentage against a player who claimed he deserved mention for the league\u2019s all-defensive team. Interestingly, the Warriors were plus-17 with Durant on the floor of that overtime game, which seems to contradict plenty, but even Durant knows he wasn\u2019t completely himself. \u201cI didn\u2019t take smart shots,\u201d he said. \u201cI took risky shots. I took some shots I know I can hit, but I\u2019d rather get better ones than that.\u201d When Durant led the Warriors to their second title in three years last summer, it was a coronation for him. He took some public grief after leaving Oklahoma City but it turned out well; the Warriors were energized by his presence, went undefeated in the Western Conference playoffs and then beat LeBron and the Cavs in five games. Plus, in that championship series he enjoyed the rarefied air of a true superstar, outplaying LeBron and winning series MVP after averaging 35.2 points on 55 percent shooting. The signature play was his go-ahead pull-up jumper from 3-point range with 45 seconds left in Game 3 , which he swished with LeBron in his face. After that experience, Durant didn\u2019t dispute the growing notion that he was one of the game\u2019s top five players, or perhaps No. 2 after LeBron. Blessed with a long wingspan, a high release point on his jumper, shooting range of 30 feet and the dexterity to execute a crossover dribble to swoop to the rim at 6-11, Durant made a compelling case. Plus, his defense vastly improved and increased his value at the other rim. Something changed since then. His 2017-18 regular season numbers were in line with his career marks, but then the Rockets stumbled upon something that stumped Durant in the West finals. He scored 37 and 38 points in the first two games, then shot 9-24, 8-22 and 6-17 over the next three. The Rockets used a switching defense to keep Durant under control. Only once in the last five postseason games has he made half his shots, and in a pair of fourth quarters he had just one basket combined. \u201cI\u2019m not worried about my makes or misses,\u201d he said. \u201cAm I getting good, quality shots? After that, the rest will take care of itself.\u201d Kerr makes a point to mention the tough shots Durant took and made in the clincher against the Rockets, yet admits this: \u201cHe knows he didn\u2019t play well in Game 1. He\u2019s excited for Game 2.\u201d Durant had the benefit of Iguodala last year and therefore didn\u2019t need to burn so much energy guarding LeBron. With Iguodala still questionable, Durant must help on LeBron, and like all the others who chipped in, failed to distinguish himself in Game 1. The Warriors are fortunate that unlike Durant, Curry is near peak form. He finished strongly against the Rockets and that carried over to the Cavs. Bouncy and frisky, Curry is on pace to cause trouble for Cleveland, with 29 points and nine assists in the opener, including a big reverse layup in the final seconds. Keep in mind that Curry\u2019s no longer worrying about guarding Kyrie Irving, and therefore isn\u2019t costing himself calories at both ends. If not for some unfortunate late-game incidents, though, the Cavs could\u2019ve beaten Golden State anyway. That\u2019s how much of a beast LeBron is, and there\u2019s the realistic possibility of little or no drop-off in production from James. Speaking of possibilities: Thompson is still wincing from a sore ankle after being undercut Thursday by Smith. He returned and played the final three quarters and overtime, but surprisingly wouldn\u2019t rule himself in for Game 2. \u201cI don\u2019t know,\u201d he said, and as for the pain? \u201cNot good.\u201d When Curry was out mending from a knee injury and Thompson went hot and cold with his shooting in the first few playoff rounds, Durant was consistently good and even great, carrying the club in scoring and delivering in close games. Can he be that player again, with the trophy on the line? \u201cWe\u2019re all a victim of expectations that we\u2019ve set,\u201d said Curry. \u201cI mean, the fact they\u2019re saying KD had a bad game is kind of funny, just with what you expect from him every night. \u201cBut we\u2019re all in this together. We know we won Game 1 by the skin of our teeth. Now we\u2019ve got to go play better, take control of the series. And KD is going to have a huge part in that, for sure.\u201d * * * Veteran NBA writer Shaun Powell has worked for newspapers and other publications for more than 25 years. Smith's impact on Finals grows","content":" OAKLAND, Calif. \u2013 Maybe J.R. Smith won\u2019t be such a goat for the Cleveland Cavaliers after all. Smith, whose mental mistake late in regulation contributed to the Game 1 defeat Cleveland suffered in overtime Thursday, was responsible for a play earlier in the night that could benefit the Cavaliers in Game 2 of The Finals . Golden State All-Star guard Klay Thompson limped and moved slowly before and after meeting with reporters Saturday, acknowledging that his left lateral leg contusion had gotten worse over the previous 24 hours. It\u2019s possible Thompson won\u2019t be able to play in Game 2 or might be limited in his effectiveness if he does. And Smith is the man who inadvertently caused Thompson to get hurt in the first place. Smith, 32, lunged for a loose ball midway through the first quarter of the series opener, but appeared to slip and slammed into Thompson\u2019s legs. The angle and impact suggested a serious injury, possibly to one of Thompson\u2019s knees, but the sharpshooting guard returned by the start of the second quarter and finished Game 1 with 24 points in 45 minutes. But Thompson\u2019s healing process, encouraging from Thursday to Friday, had reversed itself by Saturday. \u201cJust swelling, stiffness,\u201d Thompson said. \u201cI kind of expected it when the game ended because the adrenaline is not kicked in and you aren\u2019t moving as much as you were. Because right when it happens, you can play. I\u2019ve done it before. You can play on it; once you stop and go to bed that night, it kind of swells up.\u201d Thompson said he planned to do what he could in the hours leading up to Game 2 to stay loose and reduce swelling in what he\u2019s calling a high ankle sprain. He admitted that, in watching replays of the mishap, he got angry. \u201cThat\u2019s a tough play on the ball, and then just to tumble into somebody\u2019s legs like that,\u201d he said. \u201cYou\u2019ve got to move past it. It\u2019s just life, and I\u2019m going to be better from it. It\u2019s just a minor setback. But I don\u2019t think it was intentional.\u201d Reckless as Smith\u2019s maneuver was, he did show concern for Thompson as the Warriors player writhed on the court. \u201cYeah, he was remorseful, so I don\u2019t think he meant to do it. It just sucks,\u201d Thompson said. \u201cIt\u2019s a part of the game. It just sucks for the timing, during The Finals. But no one is going to feel sorry for us or me. I\u2019ve just got to do everything I possibly can these next 24 hours to be right for [Game 2].\u201d Said Smith: \u201cI was just trying to go for the ball. It looked like an opportunity for me to get a steal. Unfortunately, I didn\u2019t. I fouled him. They called the foul. I don\u2019t know what more he was looking for.\u201d No one is going to laud Smith for hurting an opposing player. But the reality is, if Thompson is hobbled and Warriors defender Andre Iguodala \u2013 listed as doubtful with a similar bone bruise suffered in the West finals \u2013 isn\u2019t available, Golden State\u2019s ability to deal with LeBron James and other Cleveland scoring threats is hampered. So if Smith can contribute in that way to an advantageous Cavs\u2019 position for Game 2, it might soothe some of the sting from Game 1. He\u2019s the guy, remember, who grabbed teammate George Hill\u2019s missed free throw late in regulation but oddly dribbled away from the basket to squander a chance to win and avoid overtime. Despite appearances, what even some Cleveland players concluded \u2013 that Smith must have thought his team was ahead, rather than tied at 107-107 at that moment \u2013 and a personal history of making similar gaffes, Smith claimed after the game that he indeed did know the score. He backed off that version Saturday. \u201cAfter thinking about it a lot the last 24 hours or however many since the game was over,\u201d Smith said, \u201cI can\u2019t say I was sure of anything at that point.\u201d Now that has the ring of truth to it for Earl Smith III. * * * Steve Aschburner has written about the NBA since 1980. Smith eager to atone for Game 1 gaffe","content":"OAKLAND, Calif. \u2014 Two days later, there is still no clarity on what J.R. Smith knew when he grabbed that offensive rebound in the final seconds of regulation of Game 1 of the NBA Finals. Not even in Smith\u2019s own mind. \u201cI can\u2019t say I was sure of anything at that point,\u201d Smith said. All he and the Cleveland Cavaliers are sure of now is this: They\u2019re down 1-0 to the Golden State Warriors in the NBA Finals, with Game 2 on Sunday night and Smith knowing that he\u2019s going to be booed, made fun of, mocked and probably worse by the crowd at Oracle Arena. Smith became one of the big Game 1 stories for all the wrong reasons. He grabbed an offensive rebound off a missed free throw in the final seconds of regulation with the game tied, then r an toward midcourt as if he was running out the clock and thinking Cleveland had a one-point lead. The Cavaliers wound up losing in overtime. \u201cHe probably took that loss as hard as anybody on the team,\u201d Cavs star LeBron James said Saturday. \u201cBut one thing about J.R., he has an uncanny ability to bounce back. I think people have seen that throughout our postseason runs, where he hasn\u2019t played well or played to his ability that he thinks he should have played, and then the next game he comes on and shoots the ball extremely well.\u201d That bounce-back tendency for Smith will be tested now like perhaps never before. This wasn\u2019t just a gaffe. This was a huge gaffe, in the final seconds of the fourth quarter of Game 1 of the NBA Finals with the game on the line. And in the immediate aftermath, it appeared Smith said to James on the court that he thought the Cavs were leading \u2014 something he denied afterward. \u201cI might have said that,\u201d Smith said. \u201cNot sure, but I might have.\u201d To say Smith was the reason Cleveland lost Thursday night isn\u2019t right, or fair. There\u2019s no guarantee that Cleveland would have scored to win the game in those final seconds if Smith had done anything differently. Still, he was blamed. \u201cI\u2019m glad it happened to me as opposed to anybody else on my team,\u201d Smith said. \u201cTo be in that situation is tough and it\u2019s not a situation everybody can handle, so I\u2019m glad it happened to me.\u201d Smith spent Friday in his hotel room for the most part, watching the Memorial (\u201cTiger played really well,\u201d Smith said), talking with Kyle Korver and visiting with his kids over FaceTime. He knows he\u2019s a punch line. He\u2019s been there before. \u201cThat\u2019s pretty much who I\u2019ve been my whole life,\u201d said Smith, who bonded with a therapy dog earlier this season during a rough stretch. \u201cI\u2019ve always been the one guy who\u2019s the butt of the jokes, or that one guy who does something crazy and everybody\u2019s got to look at or whatever the case may be. And then I come back and be myself and play the next day. I don\u2019t really dwell on things too much. It\u2019s been like that my whole life. That\u2019s what it looks like it\u2019s going to continue to be.\u201d The Cavs have made clear that they\u2019re not quitting on Smith. They need him. \u201cHe\u2019s going to start. And no, I haven\u2019t lost confidence,\u201d coach Tyronn Lue said. \u201cJ.R. can shake off anything, and when everybody tends to count J.R. out, that\u2019s when he comes through. So he\u2019s definitely going to start again. He\u2019s a big part of what we do. That last play is over, it\u2019s behind us and now we\u2019ve got to move on.\u201d A bounce-back game would be more than welcomed by Cleveland. When Smith is good, the Cavs are really good. Cleveland is 10-1 this season, including playoffs, when Smith scores more than 15 points. The Cavaliers are 19-5 when he makes more than half his shot attempts. They\u2019re 20-9 when he makes at least three 3-pointers. Those are some of the many reasons why Lue still has confidence in him, and Smith clearly appreciates that. \u201cI\u2019ve been playing like dogcrap all year and he\u2019s stuck with me,\u201d Smith said. \u201cSo he\u2019s one of the people who gives me the most confidence.\u201d And he\u2019s due, too. Smith has missed more than half his shots in eight straight games \u2014 his longest such cold streak since November. The Warriors expect that Smith and the rest of the Cavaliers will be more than ready for Game 2. And Smith said his teammates still believing in him is helping. \u201cEverybody says it\u2019s not just the one play or the reason why we lost,\u201d Smith said. \u201cTake it however you want it. My teammates have been great. Very supportive.\u201d"},{"id":40186,"link":"https:\/\/www.nba.com\/news\/cleveland-cavaliers-determined-bounce-back-crushing-game-1-loss","date":"2018-06-02","title":"Cleveland Cavaliers determined to bounce back from crushing Game 1 loss","content":"OAKLAND, Calif. \u2014 All the chatter is that underdog Cleveland could be completely deflated from the way it flopped in Game 1 of the NBA Finals. Determined and as motivated as ever might be more appropriate and fitting whenever LeBron James is in the mix. \u201cIt\u2019s one of the toughest losses I\u2019ve had in my career,\u201d James acknowledged Saturday, \u201cbecause of everything that kind of went on with the game and the way we played. Obviously, we all know what happened in the game.\u201d There were miscues aplenty, most notably J.R. Smith\u2019s offensive rebound in the final seconds of regulation that he dribbled back toward half-court in a tie game rather than shooting for a chance to win it \u2014 later insisting he knew the score, though that remains a huge mystery. The decision baffled a frustrated and stunned LeBron, who signaled at his teammate with arms pointed toward the basket. \u201cThe game\u2019s over. There\u2019s nothing we can do about it,\u201d Cavs coach Tyronn Lue said. \u201cWe\u2019ve got to move on, move forward.\u201d James has done so all postseason with an edge, intensity and ability to all-out carry the Cavs \u2014 will them to win after win, if you wish. Yes, with King James on the other side in this familiar June rivalry, Golden State knows much better than to fall into such a trap that the Cavs might be down and out, even if the defending champions have some momentum going into Game 2 on Sunday night back home at Oracle Arena. The Warriors learned that lasting lesson two years ago, when James led the Cavaliers back from the brink \u2014 a daunting 3-1 series deficit \u2014 to capture a title in Game 7 on the Warriors\u2019 home floor. \u201cI know it\u2019s not the exact same team, but we had them down 3-1 a couple years ago. They might have been deflated, and they came back and won, so we\u2019re expecting another great effort from them,\u201d Warriors coach Steve Kerr said. \u201cWe\u2019ve been through this too many times. We beat Houston on the road in Game 1 and the narrative was, you know, series is over. It seems to always be that way. There is just overreaction after a game.\u201d Draymond Green didn\u2019t sugarcoat it: Golden State got a little lucky to win Game 1 on a night James scored 51 points and the defending champions caught some big breaks. The Warriors hope to be far better with a chance to take a 2-0 series lead before the series shifts to Northwest Ohio. \u201cSometimes you need a little luck. It\u2019s good to be lucky sometimes,\u201d Green said. \u201cI\u2019ll take it.\u201d Kevin Durant wants the Warriors to remove the luck factor going forward. He even nit-picked that offensive board that Smith secured as something he should have done. \u201cAs you try to lock in on the details as much as possible, that luck factor \u2014 good luck, bad luck \u2014 you don\u2019t have it creep in if you figure out the detail parts,\u201d Durant said. \u201cTo be good at those parts of the game, then you don\u2019t let the luck creep in.\u201d Golden State gave up 19 offensive boards in all while getting only four. The Warriors know James is going to score his share of points. They just want to make it harder for him to get good looks, something that is a top priority going into Sunday. James, in his eighth straight NBA Finals and ninth overall, shot 19 for 32 to go with eight assists and eight rebounds in the opener. \u201cWe\u2019ve got to make them work harder in general,\u201d Kerr said. \u201cI thought our defense was subpar the other night.\u201d James said he is taking antibiotics and using eye drops after getting poked in the eye by Green in the first half. The outer area of James\u2019 eye was still red Saturday. Klay Thompson expects to play though is listed as questionable with what he called a sprained left ankle while Andre Iguodala remained doubtful as he works back from a bone bruise in his left knee suffered in Game 3 of the Western Conference finals against Houston and has cost him the last five games. Cleveland\u2019s J.R. Smith slid into Thompson\u2019s left leg in the first quarter of the series opener Thursday night. Thompson was dealing with stiffness, swelling and more pain Saturday. \u201cIt is a Finals game, and I\u2019m going to do everything I possibly can to play,\u201d Thompson said. \u201cIt\u2019s something you definitely don\u2019t want to have in the NBA championship.\u201d Nor does James want blood in his eye to affect his vision or alter his view of the basket. So, did he go off for 51 with only one good eye? \u201cNo, I had some points before that already,\u201d a good-natured James said, chuckling."},{"id":40182,"link":"https:\/\/www.nba.com\/news\/hang-time-podcast-nba-finals-game-2-preview","date":"2018-06-02","title":"Hang Time Podcast: NBA Finals Game 2 preview","content":"* Sunday on ABC: NBA Finals, Game 2 (8 ET) John Schuhmann joins me in Oakland to discuss the latest NBA Finals storylines before Game 2 of Cavaliers-Warriors on Sunday ( 8 ET, ABC ). * * * Subscribe to the Hang Time podcast on Apple Podcasts or iHeart Radio for a new episode after each NBA Finals game!"},{"id":40157,"link":"https:\/\/www.nba.com\/news\/klay-thompson-ankle-injury","date":"2018-06-02","title":"Golden State Warriors' Klay Thompson dealing with left ankle injury, 'optimistic' for Game 2","content":"Golden State Warriors guard Klay Thompson is dealing with a high left ankle sprain, but is expecting to play in Sunday\u2019s Game 2 of the NBA Finals. At Saturday\u2019s media availability, Thompson said the ankle was \u201cstiff\u201d but he was \u201coptimistic for tomorrow.\u201d "},{"id":40203,"link":"https:\/\/www.nba.com\/news\/finals-film-study-lebron-james-stephen-curry","date":"2018-06-02","title":"Finals Film Study: Warriors survive the James-Curry switch","content":"The last couple of rounds of these playoffs have been about switching screens and hunting mismatches. In the Western Conference finals , the Houston Rockets tried to get Stephen Curry switched onto James Harden as much as possible, so that Harden could attack Curry in isolation. In the Eastern Conference finals , the Cleveland Cavaliers tried to get Terry Rozier switched onto LeBron James, so that James could back Rozier down into the post. Appropriately, we now have the Cavs trying to get Curry switched onto James in The Finals . And if you watched the end of Game 1, you saw a lot of just that. The Cavs looked for a Curry switch onto James on their last seven possessions of regulation and their first five possessions of overtime. In total, there were 36 occasions on Thursday where the guy Curry was guarding set a screen for James or James set a screen for the guy Curry was guarding. Sometimes, the Cavs got the switch, and sometimes they didn\u2019t. And overall, the results were better for the Warriors than they were for the Cavs. On the 31 possessions in which one of those James-Curry screens were set, the Cavs scored just 31 points, a rate of exactly one point per possession. Cleveland scored 83 points (1.28 per possession) on their other 67 possessions of Game 1. Overall, it wasn\u2019t a great defensive performance for Golden State. Through the conference finals, the Warriors were the best defensive team in the postseason , allowing less than a point per possession over their 17 games. They held the San Antonio Spurs, New Orleans Pelicans and Houston Rockets well below their regular-season marks in offensive efficiency. With that in consideration, the Cavs were rather efficient in Game 1. James scored 51 points on 19-for-32 shooting, and also had eight of Cleveland\u2019s 18 assists. But it wasn\u2019t really because of all those James-Curry screens. In fact, only 23 of the Cavs\u2019 114 points (and 19 of James\u2019 51) were a direct result of a screen involving both James and Curry. Of course, 10 of those 23 points came on the last seven possessions of regulation, when the Cavs came back from a six-point deficit to \u2026 almost win. If you focus on crunch time, the James-Curry actions may have seemed more successful than they were overall. The string of 12 straight Cleveland possessions with a screen involving both James and Curry began with less than four minutes to go in regulation. And it began with the Warriors trying to avoid the switch (what they had been doing for most of the night) by having Curry \u201chedge\u201d on the screen. With J.R. Smith setting the screen for James, Curry stepped out to make James pause, so that Kevin Durant could recover\u2026 But Durant never got back in front of James, who had a direct line to the basket for a layup\u2026 From that point on, the Warriors chose to switch the screen, with the idea that a defender, even if it was Curry, would be in front of James at all times. On the very next possession, Cleveland set a staggered screen for James, with Curry as the target\u2026 Curry made the switch\u2026 But James acted quickly and got around him for a dunk\u2026 A few possessions later, there was another switch after Smith set a screen for James\u2026 When Curry turned his shoulders so that they were more parallel to the sideline, James saw an opening\u2026 He attacked and powered through Kevon Looney for an and-one that put Cleveland ahead by two. On the Cavs\u2019 final possession of regulation, with Golden State up by one, the Warriors double-teamed James for the first time in the game after George Hill (guarded by Curry) set a screen\u2026 After that, both teams were kind of scrambled. James got the ball back with Curry defending him, but with Durant and Draymond Green zoned up at the foul line\u2026 Hill smartly cut to the basket to force Klay Thompson to either leave him open for a layup or leave Kyle Korver open for a corner 3-pointer. Thompson instead fouled Hill, giving him the opportunity to win the game at the line. Of course, Hill missed the second free throw, Smith didn\u2019t know the score, the Warriors won in overtime, and the brilliance of Hill\u2019s cut was lost in the aftermath. And of course, the Cavs weren\u2019t always so successful with screens involving James and Curry as they were late in the fourth quarter. Late in the first quarter, the Cavs got a Curry-onto-James switch with a staggered screen. Initially, the Cavs weren\u2019t spaced well, allowing Nick Young to hang out in the paint\u2026 With Smith clearing out to the right wing, *James attacked once Young started moving in that direction. * A common theme here: James attacking at the exact moment a defender\u2019s movement creates a sliver of an opening. In the first example above, it was when Curry started to recover back to his man. Then, it was when Curry opened up his defensive stance. Here, it\u2019s when the help-defender (Young) starts to vacate his spot in the paint. But the Warriors collapsed into the paint before James could get into the basket, leaving three shooters open on the right side of the floor\u2026 In Game 1, the Warriors did not pay for this kind of attention on James, as his teammates shot 7-for-30 (23 percent) from 3-point range. On this possession, Jeff Green missed a three from the corner. Midway through the second quarter, the Cavs got the switch again, but it took two screens from Jordan Clarkson before the Warriors yielded, so there were only nine seconds left on the shot clock\u2026 James drove past Curry, but Durant helped off of Green to keep James from getting all the way to the basket (where he shot 9-for-10 on Thursday)\u2026 Jordan Bell was able to close out and run Green off the 3-point line. He eventually found Clarkson at the top of the key, but Clarkson had a rough night. Both the Green shot and the Clarkson shot took place with six or fewer seconds left on the shot clock. The Cavs had the ball for almost six more minutes than the Warriors did in Game 1 , and they obviously want to play more deliberately than Golden State. But according to Second Spectrum tracking, Cleveland shot 4-for-19 (including 0-for-9 from 3-point range) in the last six seconds of the shot clock. They also had a 24-second violation after a Curry switch onto James later in the second quarter. The Warriors preferred to hedge those screens in Game 1, so that Durant could continue guarding James. But the good thing about the switches was that (for the most part) it kept a defender in front of James and forced him to use more clock. (They also forced him to use more clock by fighting through an initial screen and making Cleveland re-screen to get the switch.) Cleveland probably won\u2019t shoot 4-for-19 in the last six seconds of the shot clock again. But league-wide, shooting is worse in the last six seconds (effective field goal percentage of 43 percent) than it is with 7-12 seconds left on the clock (51 percent). Every second counts. James has more than twice as many late-clock buckets as any other player in the postseason , but his teammates have not shot well in those situations . So if, after a switch, the Warriors can make him use more clock and then force the ball out of his hands, they should be pretty happy with the results. * * * John Schuhmann is a staff writer for NBA.com. The incident occurred with 2.6 seconds remaining in overtime of the Warriors\u2019 124-114 win over the Cavaliers on Thursday, May 31 at Oracle Arena. Thompson\u2019s Flagrant 2, which was assessed for his actions in contesting a jump shot by Warriors guard Shaun Livingston, was downgraded to a Flagrant 1 upon league office review. The foul occurred directly prior to Thompson\u2019s interaction with Green."},{"id":40208,"link":"https:\/\/www.nba.com\/news\/five-things-we-learned-game-1-nba-finals","date":"2018-06-02","title":"Five things we learned from unforgettable Game 1 of NBA Finals","content":"OAKLAND \u2014 Five things we learned from the Golden State Warriors\u2019 124-114 overtime victory over the Cleveland Cavaliers in Game 1 of the 2018 NBA Finals . As we get going here, let\u2019s be candid and admit that the only reason we\u2019re sticking with the \u201cFive things \u2026\u201d concept is because it\u2019s a Finals tradition around here. Fact is, we could have redubbed it \u201cFourteen things \u2026\u201d and still have had to cram in a few combo items to do justice to every crazy moment, crucial play and instance of high drama or low comedy in this one. We\u2019re starting with a macro take on the night\u2019s most hotly debated play, namely, the block\/charge call in the final minute that swung from Cleveland\u2019s advantage to Golden State\u2019s. The aftermath, in volume of commentary and in intensity, suggests we\u2019ve hit on a big-picture issue that relates not just to this Warriors-Cavaliers redux. It cuts across all sports and reaches into the culture and society, too, if we let it. The issue essentially is: Do we want to get things \u201cright?\u201d Or do we want to follow the rules? Folks incensed that the referees in Game 1 were able to go back in time and get a do-over on a judgment call when LeBron James\u2019 defensive collision with lane-attacking Kevin Durant was ruled first a charge, then overturned after replay review and labeled a blocking foul. It does not seem to matter to those upset by the process that the final outcome seemed to be correct \u2014 James had not established proper \u201cguarding position\u201d and was both moving and turning sideways at the time of contact. So what, skeptics say. It\u2019s wrong to overturn some calls and not others. Well, the referees did follow the rules, as we wrote about here . Trouble is, it\u2019s a tricky rule \u2014 a compound rule, if you will, with cascading consequences \u2014 only recently (2012-13) instituted. And it flies in the face of what preceded it by more than six decades, as far as unblowing whistles and unseeing infractions. Kind of like the use of video replays in the moment themselves. Now that it\u2019s here, though, who willingly would go back and do without it? Like air conditioning, DVRs or any other technological advancement. Here are some random thoughts on the play and its aftermath: \u2022 Everyone who says James\u2019 sneakers \u201cclearly\u201d were not in the restricted area saw a replay that confirmed it. Why shouldn\u2019t the referees, working at court level, get the same views? \u2022 Referee Ken Mauer, near the baseline with the play moving toward him, hesitated in making his charge call. It looks like he noticed colleague Tony Brothers, on the left wing, about to call James for a block. That difference in how they saw the play \u2014 besides illustrating how 50\/50 such calls can be \u2014 might have been enough, human nature intruding, to send them to the replay machinery. That, and the fact that the convergence of three pairs of feet (James\u2019, Durant\u2019s and initial defender Jeff Green\u2019s) might have planted doubt about someone stepping on the semi-circle. \u2022 People livid at Mauer for changing the call, chill. It was veteran referee Mike Callahan in the Replay Center in Secaucus, N.J., who overturned Mauer\u2019s charge call. \u2022 What\u2019s so special about block\/charge calls that they should be eligible to be overturned, based on the technical matter of feet-in-restricted area? Why is that one replay \u201ctrigger\u201d larded up with the extra options? That isn\u2019t the case on out-of-bounds plays, for instance. If someone whacks the arm of the player who last touched the ball, the refs aren\u2019t allowed to call that foul off replay. It creates the opportunity for a bogus claim about the restricted area, just to get another bite at the apple of the tough block\/charge decision. \u2022 What\u2019s so special about the final two minutes of the game that none of this applies to the first 46? The best way to address that last point: Allow. Coaches. Challenges. If both sides got two opportunities per game to ask the referees to review a play, a whole bunch of equally pivotal moments that get brushed aside through the first 95.8 percent of any game. Cleveland point guard George Hill sat for a long time after the game at his stall in the visitors\u2019 dressing room staring at his phone. He trudged around the shower\/sink area for a while, grimacing here, flailing an arm there, by himself, the other Cavs occupied with their own postgame business, his body language some mix of disgust and frustration. Hill \u2014 a career 80 percent free-throw shooter \u2014 had missed a chance with 4.7 seconds left to win Game 1, in a Finals for which he\u2019s waited his whole career. If Hill completes that simple (for him, most of the time) basketball task, J.R. Smith\u2019s subsequent brain cramp as the clock ran out in regulation, the block\/charge controversy, the skirmishes at the end of OT that might impact Game 2 \u2014 none of that matters or even happens. While Smith and Tristan Thompson took their turns standing in front of and taking questions about their antics from a glut of reporters and camera wielders in the Cavs\u2019 locker room, Hill slipped out. He got a pass on addressing his plummet from potential hero to goat. Hill expressed his feelings about the missed free throw at a media conference call Friday afternoon. We get it. Nobody in sports likes being \u201cshown up.\u201d It\u2019s understandable to not be pleased when an opponent adds an unnecessary flourish to their victory, like a pitcher staring down a batter he\u2019s just struck out or, in this case, like Golden State\u2019s Shaun Livingston firing up a shot with only a few seconds left in a game his team led by eight. But do you rumble over and foul Livingston the way Thompson did, earning himself a flagrant 2 foul and instant ejection? Brothers, the ref on that play, saw Thompson\u2019s elbow coming up high and cocking, at least from his point of view. Keep in mind, the shot clock was expiring before the game clock, and Warriors coach Steve Kerr instructs his guys to shoot rather than take intentional turnovers in such circumstances. What follows, though, is Thompson \u2014 already ejected from the game \u2014 staying on the floor long enough to push the basketball into Draymond Green\u2019s face, while making hand-to-head contact. Cue histrionics and extracurriculars, including Kevin Love appearing to come off the Cavs\u2019 bench in the kerfuffle. That\u2019s strictly prohibited, prompting some on social media to call for Love as well as Thompson to face suspensions from Game 2. In Love\u2019s case, he already had stepped on the floor, inside the 3-point line, to make some other point. So he ought not face any repercussions. Thompson? Well, he had no business still being on the court at all when he engaged Green. A swifter exit might have saved him from himself. But the larger point here is, enough with the \u201ccodes\u201d by which players (and baseball managers) temporarily lose their minds over some stupid, perceived affront to their pride in the dwindling seconds of a decided game. Pushing back against that nonsense is a waste of time and potentially detrimental to the next game. Beat them. That\u2019s it. To the victors go the spoils. Players show themselves up if they let that into their heads. If a legendary NBA player scored 51 points in nearly 48 minutes on the floor, with eight rebounds and eight assists, and everyone was too distracted or agitated to notice properly, did it happen?Like the tree falling in the proverbial forest, yes, yes it did. James turned in a Finals performance for the ages, only the sixth game of 50 points or more in NBA playoff history \u2014 and the first for a team that lost. James no doubt would have preferred to finish with 49 points and a minus-3 in plus\/minus for the game, if only Hill had scored eight points instead of seven. James was 0 for 4 with two free throws, one rebound, one block and a minus-10 in the extra five minutes. Also overshadowed: Love looking fine after missing Game 7 of the East finals at Boston and spending a week in the NBA\u2019s concussion protocol program. The Cavs forward had 21 points and 13 rebounds in 39 minutes, All-Star numbers that will need to be replicated through the series if Cleveland hopes to recover and win it. Klay Thompson\u2019s 24-point night also got neglected in most accounts, notable because most of it came after his services appeared to be lost to Golden State for the night, perhaps for the Finals. That came midway through the first quarter when Smith recklessly dove for a loose ball, slamming into Thompson\u2019s left leg and bending it in a way that screamed out \u201cACL damage!\u201d The Warriors didn\u2019t just get lucky when J.R. did his J.R thing near the end. By returning, Thompson also was able to chip in superior postgame quotage: \u201cMy spot-up ability will always gravitate the defense.\u201d This wasn\u2019t like Pete Carroll \u2014 oops! \u2014 calling a pass play for the Seahawks near the end of Super Bowl XLIX. Plenty of time here for the various miscues and controversies to be righted or forgotten in a best-of-seven series. Whether they are or not depends on the Cavaliers\u2019 resiliency vs. temptation to feel sorry for themselves. On the Warriors\u2019 understanding that they caught nearly every break and still scrambled to win at home. And on the continued healing process of Golden State\u2019s chief LeBron defender, Andre Iguodala, from the bone bruise in his left leg that kept him sidelined Thursday. James likes to call Game 1 of any series a \u201cfeel-out game,\u201d and often dials up his production from there. What, in the name of all that\u2019s holy, if he does that again? For the record, teams that win Game 1 of the Finals are 50-21 (.704) through the years at actually winning the championship. Those who snag the opener at home are 42-12 (.748). Finally, we\u2019ll exit on this, a different camera angle than the one seen on the official postgame broadcasts. Instead of cutting away to the questioner, relating info about Smith, it stays on Green in all his comedic mugging glory. # # # Steve Aschburner has written about the NBA since 1980. A missed second free throw attempt by George Hill, in his first Finals appearance, that would\u2019ve put the Cavs up a point with 4.7 seconds left in regulation? Uh-huh. James appeared to draw a charge on Durant. But officials went to the replay to see if James was in the restricted area which would make it a block. Veteran NBA game officials past and present will tell you that few plays on the court are more difficult to adjudicate than block\/charge call. The challenge of getting that play right \u2013 after first getting it wrong, apparently \u2013 was pivotal in the outcome of Golden State\u2019s 124-114 overtime victory over Cleveland in Game 1 of the 2018 Finals Thursday at Oracle Arena. In the final minute of the fourth quarter, his team trailing 104-102, Warriors forward Kevin Durant drove toward the basket with Cleveland\u2019s Jeff Green at his side. Coming in from Durant\u2019s left, LeBron James moved into his path and took the hit, falling on his back to the floor. The initial call appeared to be a charge on Durant. But in the chaos of the moment, there also seemed to be conflicting views by two of the three referees. Next thing you knew, the refs were headed to the scorer\u2019s table for a replay review. [Insert intense impatience on both sides here. Tick. Tock.] When crew chief Ken Mauer emerged from the replay huddle, in consultation with the NBA\u2019s Replay Center in Secaucus, N.J., the ruling was \u2026 Blocking foul on James. Two free throws for Durant. The frustration and disagreement was apparent on the faces of James and Cavs coach Tyronn Lue as ref Tony Brothers relayed the decision on the sideline. Durant hit both foul shots. What could have been the lead and possession of the ball for the Cavaliers instead became a tie game at 104-104, 36.4 seconds left. From there, amid further twists and turns , the game went to overtime at 107-107. And Golden State controlled those five minutes, with a 17-7 scoring edge in those five minutes that blurred what happened in the first 48. It hit hard on the Cavaliers, who felt mistreated by the review and the verdict. \u201cFor our team to come out and play their hearts out and compete the way we did, man, I mean, it\u2019s bad,\u201d Lue said. \u201cIts never been done before where you know he\u2019s outside the restricted area and then you go there and overturn the call and say it\u2019s a block. It\u2019s never been done, ever, in the history of the game.\u201d Only since the 2012-13 season, according to the NBA Official web site, have refs been permitted to review such block\/charge situations. The \u201ctrigger,\u201d to use the league\u2019s replay terminology, is uncertainty over the defensive player\u2019s position either in or outside the restricted area (that semi-circle under the rim). That\u2019s the explanation offered by Mauer late Thursday. \u201cThe reason for the trigger is that we had doubt as to whether or not James was in the restricted area,\u201d Mauer told a pool reporter. \u201cWhen over at the table, we then are allowed to determine whether or not he was in a legal guarding position. It was determined he was out of the restricted area, but he was not in a legal guarding position prior to Durant\u2019s separate shooting motion. So we had to change it to a blocking foul.\u201d Letter of the law? Guess so. But given the reluctance historically of NBA refs to un-call a foul or otherwise reverse a judgment call, the switch stood out to many as extremely rare. James did seem to turn his shoulder to Durant as he slid over, so \u201clegal guarding position\u201d might have been hard to prove. But the Cavaliers \u2013 who saw on replays that their star and leader clearly was outside the semi-circle \u2013 immediately doubted how much doubt the refs actually had. \u201cLeBron was clearly four feet outside the restricted area,\u201d Lue said. \u201cSo it doesn\u2019t make sense to go review [it].\u201d Said James, who posted an historic Finals stats line of 51 points, eight rebounds and eight assists while making 19 of 32 shots, said: \u201cI thought I read that play as well as I read any play in my career, defensively.\u201d For the Warriors, the reversal was like a reprieve from the governor. They still had time, but life would have been much more difficult had Durant been called for a charge. \u201cIt looked like a block to me,\u201d Golden State guard Klay Thompson said. There still were misadventures to come. Cleveland\u2019s George Hill got to the line at 107-106 and 4.7 seconds left. An 80 percent foul shooter, Hill made his first free throw. Then missed his second. But wait, there\u2019s more: Cavs gunner J.R. Smith slipped in to grab the miss. Only, instead of going right back up for a shot, Smith dribbled to the right wing. Even as James implored Smith to pass him the ball or head toward the basket, the veteran shooting guard shoveled the ball to Hill in the right corner. Hill\u2019s awkward fling looked late and wasn\u2019t close as time expired. The way it looked, Smith seemed to think his team had the lead. Not true, he said. \u201cI tried to bring it out and get enough space to maybe get a shot off,\u201d he said. \u201cIf I thought we were ahead, I would have just held onto the ball and let them foul me.\u201d Said Lue: \u201cHe thought it was over. He thought we were up one.\u201d Oh, and as something to enflame emotions and liven up media coverage before Game 2 Sunday, there was a kerfuffle in the final seconds involving Tristan Thompson, Shaun Livingston, Draymond Green, violations of various \u201ccodes\u201d and unwritten rules and some likely verdicts issued from NBA headquarters in the next 72 hours. But for Cleveland, as frustrated as the Cavs might have been with Smith, as bummed as they were over Hill\u2019s missed free throw and as badly as it stung to squander James\u2019 masterful performance, the block\/charge overturn was the one that stuck. That\u2019s the one that\u2019ll leave a mark. \u201cMan, it ain\u2019t right,\u201d Lue said. \u201cIt ain\u2019t right.\u201d * * * Steve Aschburner has written about the NBA since 1980. The views on this page do not necessarily reflect the views of the NBA, its clubs or Turner Broadcasting."},{"id":40229,"link":"https:\/\/www.nba.com\/news\/adam-silver-weighs-cap-system-league-parity-and-more","date":"2018-06-01","title":"NBA Commissioner Adam Silver weighs in on cap system, league parity and more","content":" OAKLAND, Calif. \u2013 The two teams that have pitched tents on the NBA Finals for the past four years share a bunch of attributes. Star power, 3-point marksmanship, continuity at least at some minimal level, crafty and dedicated coaching staff, and a know-how that has grown with each subsequent championship showdown. There\u2019s one other thing, though, that should not be overlooked. The Golden State Warriors and the Cleveland Cavaliers rank Nos. 1 and 2 in team payroll, each spending a few exotic motor cars north of $137 million on the players competing in the 2018 Finals. Warriors guard Steph Curry ($34.7 million) and Cleveland forward LeBron James ($33.3 million) are the league\u2019s two highest paid stars this season, and they have a combined total of seven teammates each being paid more than $15 million in 2017-18. It\u2019s no coincidence, then, that in terms of on-court success, the Golden State and Cleveland owners are getting what they paid for. Or, perhaps inversely, paying for what they get. This bang-for-the-big-bucks aspect came up Thursday in NBA commissioner Adam Silver\u2019s annual pre-Finals news conference. Silver was talking about the oddity of the same two teams meeting in the championship round for the fourth consecutive time \u2013 unprecedented across the four major U.S. pro team sports. The fact is, beyond talent and strategies, being able and willing to spend top dollar undeniably is a major factor. That goes right to the heart of the league\u2019s economic model. What gives? The salary cap gives, that\u2019s what. Unlike the \u201chard\u201d cap system used by the NFL, the NBA has a \u201csoft\u201d cap that makes it painful and pricey, but not impossible, for a team owner to exorbitantly overpay for success. The official cap figure this season was $99 million, with a $119.2 million luxury-tax threshold. Both the Warriors and the Cavaliers payouts dwarf those numbers. The difference between hard and soft is one reason the NFL is known for its parity while the NBA is dealing these days with two dominant franchises and 28 wannabes.  \u201cYou still have two teams that are significantly above, not just the cap, but the tax,\u201d Silver said. \u201cNow [the hard cap is] something that we\u2019ll continue to look at. There are pros and cons to doing it. Historically, one of the issues in our league was we didn\u2019t necessarily want to break up teams. There is a different sense in the NBA than the NFL, and the chemistry and dynamic that comes together with a group of players.\u201d Any changes in the cap system would need to be collectively bargained, of course, with the National Basketball Players Association. \u201cYou could do more to achieve parity, but you also don\u2019t want parity of mediocrity either,\u201d Silver said. The NBA wants its Larry O\u2019Brien Trophy to still celebrate excellence, not participation. And excellence isn\u2019t only a function of finance. \u201cNot to take anything away from strong coaching and general managing and ownership,\u201d Silver said, \u201cbut you have certain players in our league that are so transcendent that their team is always probably going to be in the hunt.\u201d Take this for data, from the commissioner via a story on TheRinger.com. \u201cI looked this up: LeBron, now over the course of the last eight Finals, has played with 59 different players,\u201d Silver said. \u201cIt means that presumably the team LeBron is on is going to be a dominant team.\u201d So to get back to the original question: Is it a good thing for the NBA to have the same two teams in the Finals four years in a row? \u201cThe greater the competition you can create, the greater their interests will be,\u201d Silver said at one point. \u201cAt the same time, I don\u2019t think it\u2019s inconsistent to celebrate greatness and to say, my God, look what these two teams are doing.\u201d As usual, Silver fielded questions on a variety of topics, including: \u2014 Regarding the furor in Philadelphia in which chief basketball exec Bryan Colangelo is accused of using multiple cloaked Twitter accounts to anonymously take on critics, defend his reputation and even question players\u2019 performances and off-court activities, Silver deferred to an independent investigation undertaken by the Sixers. \u201cHere we are, Game 1 of The Finals, it\u2019s not necessarily something we want to be talking about,\u201d the commissioner said, \u201cbut it\u2019s the reality of this league. So I have no information beyond that other than that investigation is underway.\u201d \u2014 As the league embraces the looming prospect of legalized sports betting, one issue is the NBA\u2019s expectation of a royalty or \u201cintegrity fee\u2019\u2019 \u2013 1 percent of all bets made is an estimate floated by some \u2013 to compensate the league for its intellectual property and\/or the costs of keeping competition on the up-and-up. Some gaming enterprises already have balked at the idea. \u201cTheir view is we\u2019re not entitled to that integrity fee,\u201d Silver said. \u201cAnd my response to those gaming organizations is, \u2018If that\u2019s your view, then we will negotiate with you directly.\u2019 Because I think many of them desire to get official data feeds from the league. They desire to use our trademarks for placing bets.\u201d \u2014 One-and-done might not be around much longer. That eligibility rule for players to enter the Draft could give way to the league\u2019s development league (G League), nearly a 30-to-30 affiliate system now, and its use of two-way contracts to move players up and down as needed. A wrinkle in the debate pertains to a study chaired by former Secretary of State Condoleezza Rice that asserted one-and-done prospects might be more trouble \u2013 in the form of recruiting and eligibility infractions \u2013 than they\u2019re worth. Said Silver: \u201cTheir view that players at 18 who are of NBA caliber \u2026 should not be in college and they should be playing either directly in the NBA or in our development league. And I will say that if you have, in essence, college saying, \u2018We don\u2019t want these players, it would be hard for us not to respond.\u201d \u2014 A recurring cry to reduce the NBA\u2019s regular season from 82 games, allegedly to cut down on injuries, got no traction with the commissioner. He noted that some of this season\u2019s most debilitating injuries \u2013 such as season-enders suffered by San Antonio\u2019s Kawhi Leonard and New York\u2019s Kristaps Porzingis \u2013 came after those players had appeared in very few games. \u201cIf we had any data, hard data suggesting that a season that was 75 games or 72 games instead of 82 games would reduce injuries other than just playing fewer games,\u201d Silver said, \u201cwe\u2019d be taking a hard look at shortening our season. We just don\u2019t have that data right now.\u201d * * * Steve Aschburner has written about the NBA since 1980. You can e-mail him here , find his archive here and follow him on Twitter . The Warriors\u2019 attack was more balanced, with Stephen Curry (29 points), Kevin Durant (26) and Klay Thompson (24) leading the way. Both teams led the game by 11 points at one point, but this one went down to the wire. One stat stood out from the Warriors\u2019 124-114 overtime victory . The stat  6 \u2014 Consecutive possessions the Warriors scored on to start overtime. The context  After building a six-point lead with less than five minutes to go in regulation, the Warriors got stagnant, going to Durant and Curry isolations and scoring on just one of their next five possessions to allow the Cavs to take a two-point lead. But a reversal of a block\/charge call, a Curry drive, and J.R. Smith\u2019s mental error in the closing seconds allowed the Warriors to get to overtime, where they found themselves \u2014 and found Thompson. On their first possession of OT, Durant backed George Hill down late in the clock and drew a foul. Then, off a Cleveland miss, Draymond Green found Thompson circling to the right corner for a three, his first field goal attempt since the 10:07 mark of the fourth quarter. One possession later, Curry ran pick-and-roll, got into the paint to draw an extra defender, and tossed the ball over his head to Shaun Livingston to put the Warriors up seven. Then, when the Cavs got mixed up on an off-ball switch, Green found Livingston cutting to the basket for a dunk. Green saved the Warriors with a buzzer-beating three on the next possession, and then Green slipped to the basket, caught a Curry pass, and found Thompson in the left corner for another three that put the champs up 11 with just a minute to go. Going into The Finals, the Warriors ranked second (behind Philadelphia) in both ball movement (361 passes per 24 minutes of possession) and player movement (10.9 miles traveled per 24 minutes of possession) in the playoffs, according to Second Spectrum tracking . After struggling through some stagnant offense late in the fourth, bodies moved and the ball moved in overtime, where the Warriors assisted on all five of their buckets. They finished with 31 assists and just seven turnovers on the night. Thompson finished 5 of 10 from 3-point range. Brown, who has served as the team\u2019s head coach since 2013, will be under contract with the 76ers through the 2021-22 season. \u201cBrett has done a terrific job over the past several years building and cultivating a program, but the substantially positive growth in the win column these past two seasons has proven he is the right man to continue leading this team in our quest for an NBA championship,\u201d Colangelo said. \u201cI couldn\u2019t be more thrilled for Brett, his family and the organization to be moving forward for several years to come.\u201d Under Brown\u2019s leadership, Philadelphia has increased its win total by 42 games since the start of the 2015-16 season, a run that tied Boston (2006-08) for the largest turnaround within a three-year span in NBA history. \u201cBrett has done a remarkable job in helping build a family-like culture centered around player development, work ethic and a commitment to long-term winning. With this contract extension, we look forward to the 76ers\u2019 continued growth under his leadership. He has helped develop some of the NBA\u2019s brightest young stars, and we\u2019re excited for what awaits as we continue to add to this talented roster,\u201d said Harris Blitzer Sports & Entertainment and Philadelphia 76ers Managing Partner David Blitzer. The 76ers finished the 2017-18 season with a record of 52-30, good for third place in the Eastern Conference. The third-place finish was their best since finishing first in 2000-01 and marked the team\u2019s first postseason appearance since 2012. Brown led the team to a 4-1 series win over the sixth-seeded Miami Heat in the first round of the 2018 NBA Playoffs. The series victory marked the first time Philadelphia won a best-of-seven playoff series in five-or-fewer games since sweeping Milwaukee in 1985. The 76ers also set a new franchise playoff record, converting on 18 three-pointers in Games 1 and 3 against Miami; the previous team best was 11, which had been accomplished twice. Brown, the Eastern Conference Coach of the Month for March\/April, guided the 76ers to 16 straight wins to end the season, which set an NBA record for the longest winning streak by a team heading in the playoffs. The 16-game winning streak is a single-season team record. \u201cI am especially grateful to my coaching staff and my players,\u201d said Brown. \u201cIt takes a village. Under Brown\u2019s direction, the team finished ninth in offensive rating, posted the most home wins in a season since 1988-89 (30) and advanced to the Eastern Conference Semifinals. Philadelphia posted an Eastern Conference-best 27.1 assists per game, a mark topped only by the defending NBA champion Golden State Warriors (29.3). It was the 76ers\u2019 best such mark in a single season since posting 27.6 per contest in 1981-82. From March 16-April 1, the 76ers posted at least 30 assists in nine straight games. The nine-game streak was a franchise record and is tied for the third-longest such streak in league history. With an overall defensive rating of 102.0, the 76ers ranked third in the NBA and second in the Eastern Conference. The only teams with lower defensive ratings than Philadelphia, were Boston (101.5) and Utah (101.6), which both qualified for the NBA Playoffs. Anchoring the defense were NBA All-Defensive selections forward Robert Covington (First Team) and center Joel Embiid (Second Team). Covington, Embiid, forward Dario \u0160ari\u0107, guard JJ Redick and guard-forward Ben Simmons made up the team\u2019s most frequently-used starting lineup, which held a record of 32-15 in its 47 games together. This lineup outscored its opponents by a league-leading 269 points on the season. No other five-man lineup outscored its opponents by more than 169 points. Simmons was selected to the All-Rookie First Team last week. Coach Brown has had at least one player selected to the All-Rookie First Team in each of his five seasons at the helm. Under Brown\u2019s tutelage, Embiid blossomed into a first-time All-Star starter in 2018, while Simmons produced a historic rookie season as the team\u2019s primary ball handler, posting 12 triple-doubles, the second-most ever by a rookie. Houston is the next closest team with 3,588, while the closest Eastern Conference foe is Atlanta, which has posted 3,481 steals over the past five seasons. Brown, an ambassador to youth coaches in the Philadelphia and Camden communities, hosts an annual coaching clinic, which is a part his Brett Brown Coaches Circle initiative that provides outreach and resources to local basketball coaches. Bounce Out the Stigma is a nonprofit organization that holds basketball camps for youth with unique needs in 20 states designed to empower children. I know a lot of people in the Bay Area think this is going to go on forever. On the record, it won\u2019t. \u201cIt can\u2019t. Nothing does,\u201d Myers continued. \u201cEspecially in a sport where the competition is so great. There\u2019s financial pressures. There\u2019s all kinds of things \u2013 injuries, personalities, everything, that don\u2019t allow you to\u2026 No team should last forever. It\u2019s not good for anybody.\u201d  Fans of the Warriors and the Cavaliers would argue otherwise, but there is a reason what we\u2019re seeing now \u2013 two franchises butting heads for the fourth consecutive time in a championship round \u2013 never has happened in the four major U.S. professional sports. Staying on top, holding teams together, overcoming injuries, coping with age and egos and constant challenges from a field of rivals can wear even on a champion. Notice how both the Warriors and the Cavaliers worked through less-than-fulfilling regular seasons and had to overcome 2-3 deficits in the conference finals to reach this stage this time. \u201cI didn\u2019t need a reminder from Houston to know how fragile this whole thing is,\u201d Myers said. \u201cThat\u2019s part of it. That\u2019s why you\u2019ve got to appreciate it. The notion that these things go on forever? At one point, players get older. Teams get broken up. It always happens. You never know when.\u201d Invariably, that sizes up an executive in Myers\u2019 position for a black hat. He is the one charged with renewal, which often means shedding players with whom fans have fallen in love through the high times. Or maybe Myers ends up as the bad guy because he doesn\u2019t refresh the roster soon enough and a team of former champs gets old together. \u201cMy day\u2019s coming at some point,\u201d said Myers, one of the NBA\u2019s acclaimed \u201cgolden boys\u201d through this Warriors run. \u201cEverybody gets hammered for something they do or not. It\u2019s part of the deal. Who knows? You\u2019d like to treat the players with respect \u2013 they\u2019ve earned it. Our fans are very important. You just do the best you can. \u201cI had no idea we were going to win any championships. \u2026 Every decision, signing players, keeping a team together is difficult. Not keeping a team together is difficult. Making trades, not making trades. It\u2019s all part of it.\u201d So give it time. Change will come. Now, for how that relates to this Finals and its opponents, so familiar with each other and to the world, the real question ought to be: What was the alternative? Should Cleveland and Golden State have laid down and gotten out of the way of new blood just \u2026 because? \u201cIt may not be as suspenseful as a lot of people want it to be or as drama-filled, but that\u2019s what you\u2019ve got movies and music for,\u201d Golden State\u2019s Kevin Durant said. \u201cI think this is a great display of basketball on the court from both sides, and if you\u2019re a real lover of the game, you can enjoy how both teams play it, even though it may be different. \u201cIt\u2019s still organic and true to the game, pure to the game. So if you enjoy basketball, I don\u2019t feel like you should have any complaints because it\u2019s a great set of players on both teams.\u201d Cleveland star LeBron James was more blunt and business-focused. A Rockets-Celtics Finals might offer new storylines and fresh faces, but there\u2019s always appeal \u2013 and marketability \u2013 to the defending champions taking on the game\u2019s best player, again. And again. \u201cYou\u2019ve got to ask Adam Silver,\u201d James said, referencing the NBA commissioner as the fellow whose opinion matters most on the sameness of this Finals. Then he elaborated. \u201cTeams have had their opportunities to beat the Cavs over the last four years, and teams have had the opportunities to beat the Warriors over the last four years,\u201d James said. \u201cIf you want to see somebody else in the postseason, then you got to beat them.\u201d Warriors guard Klay Thompson echoed that view. \u201cI think the rest of the NBA has got to get better,\u201d he said. \u201cIt\u2019s not our fault. \u201cThe only people I hear saying that are fans from other teams, which is natural. I don\u2019t blame them. But as long as our fan base is happy, that\u2019s all that matters.\u201d * * * Steve Aschburner has written about the NBA since 1980.
