The views on this page do not necessarily reflect the views of the NBA, its clubs or Turner Broadcasting."},{"id":40425,"link":"https:\/\/www.nba.com\/news\/morning-tip-qa-stephen-curry-golden-state-warriors","date":"2018-05-21","title":"Morning Tip Q&A: Stephen Curry","content":" They don\u2019t wear it, Stephen Curry and Steve Kerr. Each is grounded, given more to cracking a joke than cussing someone out. Each has a well-earned reputation as a well-mannered, thoughtful guy. But each hides, under the surface, a bubbling cauldron, a nasty streak\u2014what used to be called the \u201cred ass\u201d\u2014that comes out at peak moments of competition, of anger, of passion. Kerr cracks the occasional clipboard. And so it was that after Steph Curry hit his seventh or eighth straight shot in the third quarter of what became a record-breaking 41-point Game 3 demolition of the Rockets, culminating a 96-hour stretch during which Curry\u2019s 2 of 13 three-point shooting combined in Games 1 and 2 against Houston had been analyzed and massaged and hot taked to within an inch of his life, but had been silenced by Curry\u2019s 35-point smackdown in response, that Curry took out his mouthpiece and yelled, \u2018this is my (bleeping) house\u201d , not that anyone could hear him amid the bedlam at Oracle Arena. Afterward, he said his mother, Sonya, had chided him and said he needed to wash out his mouth with soap. But while the outburst was profane (\u201cRiley, I hope you weren\u2019t watching,\u201d fellow Splash Brother Klay Thompson joked in the locker room, referring to Curry\u2019s 5-year-old daughter), it wasn\u2019t especially out of character. Curry is a figurative killer on the floor, a destroyer of opponent worlds who wants to win, bad, and who wants to beat back the challenge that Houston, which won 65 games this season , the most in the league, and that hasn\u2019t been shy about its dogged pursuit of the defending champions. You don\u2019t win two league MVP awards by age 28 if you aren\u2019t a competitive little cuss. It\u2019s how Curry got back in five weeks from a Grade 2 MCL sprain of his left leg to return to the Warriors for their second-round win over New Orleans. It\u2019s what keeps him from being embarrassed as the Rockets put him in endless screen and rolls, making him guard either James Harden or Chris Paul one on one. (He hasn\u2019t done badly at all against them, even as the effort he\u2019s had to put out on defense could well have sapped his legs offensively). When he sat down before Game 3, he displayed no true concern about his knee or his shot\u2014the knee having been worked out strenuously by the Warriors\u2019 medical and training staffs, along with Kerr\u2019s personal trainer, Brandon Payne, during Curry\u2019s rehab. \u201cI\u2019m convinced he\u2019s 100 percent,\u201d Payne said Sunday morning. Curry convinced the rest of us a little later, using the derivative adverb form. Me: So, totally removed from this: I heard you killed it when your team made its presentation to buy the Panthers. Stephen Curry: That\u2019s still kind of an ongoing process. Very eye opening process to be a part of. Me: What was different in that process as opposed to coming at things as a player? SC: I think just understanding the full scope of what the business side means. It was an opportunity to understand how to run a franchise\u2014not to run a franchise\u2014but the background, the details, all the different hands at play, especially when it comes to Charlotte, being the city that I grew up in. So understanding more so about how the community and the fan base, how it\u2019s interacting with the organization, the upside of what that could mean down the road, obviously partnering with the NFL, how the NFL is growing from a fan and a game interaction type of standpoint, there\u2019s a lot going on when you think about owning a franchise. Pretty educational experience. Me: Do you get, almost, offended when someone asks you\u2019re having a problem shooting or if something\u2019s wrong after a bad game or two? SC: That\u2019s a strong word. I find it amusing, because, one, there\u2019s nothing that anybody can ask me or tell me that I haven\u2019t already been thinking about myself when it comes to how I\u2019m playing, what I need to do better. The consistent thing is I\u2019ll never lose confidence in myself when I\u2019m out there on the floor. That\u2019s why I shoot the shots that I do; that\u2019s why I play the way that I play. Beyond that, it\u2019s part of the game. And I have high expectations for myself, and I know I set lofty goals when it comes to shooting the basketball. And if I don\u2019t meet that standard every night there\u2019s going to be questions about why this, why that. We\u2019re in the Western Conference finals. We have to play well for us to get to that next level. Me: Houston\u2019s got a really good defense, but while they have good size on the wings and especially with Clint Capela in the back, they\u2019re not an overly long team. What do they do so well defensively? SC: I think it\u2019s just their switching. They try to interrupt your flow by putting a body at every interaction, every screen, between the ball, off the ball. It\u2019s what we\u2019ve done for years, where we try just to kill the flow. Not allow as many passes or ball movement. They have pretty good versatility. They may not be the longest group from top to bottom, but the versatility of having guys that can guard multiple positions, that helps them. They\u2019re tough, and obviously, what Clint Capela can do in the paint, protecting the rim. I feel like it\u2019s just one of those things where it\u2019s a matter of whose will is going to win over the course of seven games. Me: Does it feel weird that you guys did what you were supposed to do\u2014win one on the road\u2014and yet before Game 3 it was all gloom and doom because of what happened in Game 2? SC: It\u2019s probably two things. One, for us as a team, we haven\u2019t been in the experience of being on the road to start a series. That dynamic is different. It\u2019s kind of been our pattern, in most series we play in, we win the first two games at home, go on the road and split, and come back and win Game 5. So if you just reverse the pattern, we\u2019re right where we want to be. It\u2019s just a bad feeling to win Game 1 the way we did, how well we played, and Game 2 happens, they came out with force and we don\u2019t answer it. It wasn\u2019t like, from our standpoint, it wasn\u2019t anything like the game plan or much different besides a couple of momentum plays where we gave them confidence and they took it and ran with it. A lot of guys played well. It\u2019s a weird feeling but we know when we get back to locking in on both sides of the ball, paying attention to what they do well and try to stop it, play off of the home crowd, we should be in good shape. Me: Are you at this time of the year communicating with Brandon (Payne) or the other guys on your personal team to see if they see anything, or do you say \u2018I\u2019ve got this; I don\u2019t want to hear from you?\u2019 SC: A little bit of both. I worked out with Brandon before we left on the road trip. For the most part, there\u2019s noise all over the place. As long as I\u2019m locked down with my guys here in the locker room and on the practice court, I should be in good shape. It\u2019s rare that what I do has to fold into what we\u2019re doing as a team. And obviously, the only other thing somebody\u2019s gotta tell me is \u2018you have to play better.\u2019 Me : Did anybody do that? SC: Oh, I\u2019ve got plenty of people who do that, shoot it with me straight. Me: Who are the ones you listen to? SC: My wife, and probably my parents the most. \u2018Cause they\u2019re the ones at my house. (Laughs) Me: Why is it that when your team struggles, it always shows up with turnovers? It\u2019s not like you miss a bunch of free throws or play horrible defense; it\u2019s always a bunch of turnovers. SC: I think the story of Game 2 was the first quarter and the seven turnovers we had. They weren\u2019t ones that were aggressive; they were all passive, being indecisive, rushing. That set the tone for how we played at the offensive end. We had a bunch of defensive breakdowns, but I think our morale was killed early; we didn\u2019t have the right juice or mojo. I don\u2019t know what it is about that specific part of the game. We try to involve a lot of ball movement, and there\u2019s a lot of organized chaos in how we run our transition offense and stuff like that. But we have smart players who know how to take care of the ball. It\u2019s just a bad habit to break, and you have to do it if you want to be great. Me: Is Houston the biggest challenge to you in the West during this run? SC: I mean, we were down 3-1 against the Thunder in the same series. Think about where we are right now at this point. It\u2019s a different conversation because we started on the road, All the noise around Houston throughout the course of the season and being on a colliding path (with them) all year. There\u2019s a lot built into hyping this series, and it should be. Me: You\u2019ve got this chance to repeat, you got relatively healthy as a team. Nobody\u2019s repeated since Miami. On the list of things you can accomplish, does that have any bearing or impact? SC: I think it does, because if we win it this year, that\u2019s what that means. It\u2019s something we\u2019ve never done before. This is our second crack at it. Historically, throughout the years, I can\u2019t remember the exact number, but I know it\u2019s single digits how many teams have repeated in the league. We know how hard it is. We always talk about San Antonio, a team that won five of them but never two in a row. It\u2019s totally different circumstances you have to overcome to win a championship one time, much less two in a row. We understand the moment. That\u2019s how we got to this point. But, yeah, that means something. We know how hard it is, and it\u2019s a challenge we haven\u2019t accomplished. Me: I\u2019m trying to come up with the proper way to ask this. There was another school shooting Friday, this one in Houston. When you have to be locked in as much as you have to be this time of year, is it difficult to process your feelings about tragedies like that\u2014because you have to be human. Do you allow that to impact you the way it would normally? SC: That\u2019s a great question. I\u2019ve been in this situation, playing basketball. It\u2019s my job. It\u2019s what I love to do. In the grand scheme of life, win, lose or draw, it\u2019s not the end of the world. When I get to the playoffs, I try to shut out as much as possible. This is a three-month period that means a lot to me and the people around me, and I want to be locked in during that time. But life doesn\u2019t stop for us. When you hear about stories like the Santa Fe shooting, or any type of social unrest, you can\u2019t be numb to it. You have to have that process of talking about it with your family. Everybody has a different way of dealing with those types of situations. To me, you want to be able to let life happen, but understand when it\u2019s time to play, it doesn\u2019t mean I can\u2019t give every bit of my attention and effort or whatever to the court. But make sure that people who depend on me for that type of emotional support (after tragedy) that I\u2019m available for them. Me: The Rockets seem to be willing to live with KD isoed at the elbow. What\u2019s the sweet spot between letting him attack and everybody touching the ball and the flow, because that\u2019s what you guys do? SC: We\u2019ve done it before. I think it\u2019s just a matter of not losing our identity because of how fast we\u2019re playing, creating those possessions when the ball is moving from side to side and everybody\u2019s touching it and being aggressive. What Houston did in Game 1 and Game 2 was Trevor Ariza, Eric Gordon, P.J. Tucker, they got the ball in their hands and they made plays. We\u2019ve got guys like that, as well\u2014Andre (Iguodala), Klay, Draymond (Green), myself, Shaun (Livingston) as well. DWest coming off the bench. As long as we all get our touches and be aggressive, everybody\u2019s looking to score, KD\u2019s going to get his. I\u2019m going to get mine, everybody\u2019s going to get touches and the opportunity to make a play. Me: So, if you get three simultaneous messages from Sonya Curry, Dell Curry and Ayesha Curry that you\u2019re not playing that well, which one do you pay the most attention to? SC: Oh, my wife, for sure. My wife, for sure. Because I would never, ever want to disappoint her. She\u2019s supportive. She shoots it to me straight, that\u2019s the amazing thing, whether I play well or not. I don\u2019t get too big a head or too down on myself. \u2013Lakers rookie Kyle Kuzma ( @kylekuzma ), Friday, 8:11 p.m., throwing IT sized shade at his (currently) Lakers teammate. \u201cI really wanted to get up and leave at that point and demand a recount.\u201d \u2013Mavericks Vice President of Basketball Operations Michael Finley,  to the Dallas Morning News , after the Mavs fell from third to fifth in the upcoming Draft following last week\u2019s Lottery. \u201cThere are some things that you are going to have to give up to be a No. 1, No. 2 team in field-goal percentage defense. The views on this page do not necessarily reflect the views of the NBA, its clubs or Turner Broadcasting."},{"id":40408,"link":"https:\/\/www.nba.com\/news\/boston-celtics-need-al-horford-steady-presence-game-4-preview","date":"2018-05-21","title":"Al Horford needs to rock if Boston Celtics are to roll in Game 4","content":" * On ESPN: Celtics vs. Cavs, Game 4 (8:30 ET) CLEVELAND \u2014 Marcus Morris nominated himself again as Boston\u2019s lightning rod as the Celtics try to slow the Cleveland Cavaliers\u2019 roll in Game 4 of the Eastern Conference finals on Monday night. Al Horford, meanwhile, has got to rock. Rock, as in be there for the Celtics in whatever and however many ways they need him. And they most definitely need him. Rock, as the steadying veteran presence Horford is, in the eyes of virtually everyone who watches Boston play or has a sense of that roster\u2019s chemistry. Rock, as in the player his coaches and his teammates lean on precisely at times such as this, regardless of what\u2019s gone on before. The back story of Horford vs. LeBron James and the Cavaliers in the playoffs is not a pretty one. In four previous encounters \u2014 in 2009, 2015 and 2016 while with Atlanta, in 2017 after signing with Boston \u2014 Horford\u2019s teams went 1-16. For all of his individual and group success to that point, including four All-Star selections, Horford averaged just 10.9 points and 4.3 rebounds in the four series. He had an average plus\/minus of minus-53 per series, according to basketball-reference.com. The narrative changed as the current East finals began. Horford scored 20 points in less than 28 minutes of Game 1 , helping Boston to a blowout victory. Even with Cleveland coach Tyronn Lue tabbing center Tristan Thompson to match up with Horford in Game 2  \u2014 Thompson had a history of strong defense against the Celtics veteran \u2014 Horford managed 15 points, 10 rebounds, two steals and two blocks as Boston won again. In two games, he shaved 31 points off that negative plus\/minus. This was the Horford people on both sides of the equation, his teams and the other guys, had been lauding for years. Ask any of them about the 6-foot-10 son of former NBA player Tito Horford and you\u2019d wind up with what sounded like blurbs for the back cover of a best seller: \u201cAl\u2019s important to everything we do,\u201d Celtics coach Brad Stevens said after Game 1. \u201cEvery minute of every day, whether it\u2019s the walk-through before, the meeting before, yesterday\u2019s practice, yesterday\u2019s film session. Again, he\u2019s kind of our stabilizer and the guy that we all look to and we take great comfort that he\u2019s on our team.\u201d James, before the series began, said: \u201cHe\u2019s their rock. [He] always is even keel. \u2026 He\u2019s been in big games, in big series. If things get chaotic, they know they can always go to Al to do something.\u201d Cavs shooter Kyle Korver, who became an All-Star himself in 2015 alongside Horford on Atlanta\u2019s 60-22 team, said: \u201cAl is the ultimate teammate. Doesn\u2019t say a lot, so when he says something it means something. \u2026 He\u2019s like a Swiss army knife \u2014 he does everything. The thing that\u2019s stood out for me in these playoffs is how aggressive he\u2019s been to score \u2026 just because he knows the team needs it a little more right now.\u201d After averaging 12.9 points in the regular season, Horford had upped his scoring to 17.1 through the Celtics\u2019 first 14 playoff games. What he had gone through in the past against James and the Cavaliers seemed to add context and urgency, and a chance for Horford finally to make things right. Then came Game 3, when Horford took only four shots and scored seven points. His drop in production was a symptom of what ailed Boston Saturday more than a cause. Thrown by the Cavaliers\u2019 torrid start, the Celtics stopped moving the ball and settled too often for low-reward, pull-up jump shots. Still, without Kyrie Irving, Horford has been the proverbial head of Boston\u2019s snake, so stopping him for the 30 minutes he played made Cleveland\u2019s wire-to-wire rout easier. \u201cThe ball was kind of stagnant last game,\u201d Morris said. \u201cWe didn\u2019t get it from side to side. Stephen Curry's (expletive) house","content":" OAKLAND, Calif. \u2014 It was a moment, to be frozen in time, chilling to witness and stunning to fully understand when it burst upon an unsuspecting audience with gale force. A sequence of dribbling, a drive to the hoop followed by a delicate finger roll for a bucket and then, boom: Steph Curry screamed a bad word. Yes, he did. The religious family man with the innocent face and made-for-Disney image reached deep into his Forbidden Dictionary and found something naughty. Was this a first for him in public view? After a quick first-step left Trevor Ariza choking on exhaust and when he finished at the rim in the third quarter, Curry had an out of body experience and temporarily exhumed himself from Game 3 . While the action moved up-court, he turned and faced the Oracle Arena crowd \u2014 now standing and pulsating and pleading for more, more, more! \u2014 and removed his trusty mouthpiece, pointed both index fingers to the ground and bellowed, with attitude: \u201cThis is my freaking house!\u201d Only he substituted another, ahem, adjective for \u201cfreaking.\u201d He acknowledged afterward, regrettably: \u201cI know, I know. I blacked out.\u201d Point made, though. This was his house, his fans, his game, his turnaround performance of the playoffs and who knows, this could end up being his series as a result. Normally the most vivid expression anyone gets from Curry after a made basket is a playful shimmy shake, which happened on the previous possession, when he isolated on James Harden and splashed a 30-footer. Yes, that shot was satisfying and worth a wiggle. But Curry was feeling it. And he hadn\u2019t felt it for some time, since before the playoffs, since before the knee injury that kept him out for six weeks left him rusty. So there was emotional release coming that was cutting edge for him. This cursing was the sound of relief, from the frustration of all that, a two-time MVP hailed as the finest shooter of our generation suddenly bricking shots from 3 -point range in the Western Conference finals and becoming A Rather Big Topic here against the Rockets. And so \u2026 maybe forgiveness for the potty mouth is in order? Here\u2019s why: Curry was three-for-20 beyond the 3-point stripe in the first two-and-a-half games against Houston, but the script flipped suddenly and sharply in Sunday\u2019s third quarter. He found himself, his stroke, his place in the pecking order, and he kept rolling with it. He hit layups, mid-range jumpers and yes, 3s, and they all came in a rush, a flood that engulfed the Rockets. \u201cWe\u2019ve seen this so many times with Steph,\u201d coach Steve Kerr said. \u201cAll it takes is one. He bounces back from bad games as well as anyone I\u2019ve ever seen. I was never concerned.\u201d He finished with 35 points, including 26 in the second half and the Warriors romped by 41. They\u2019re up 2-1 now, with another home game coming Tuesday. The Warriors have won their last 16 home playoff games, an NBA record. They also just held the high-wire Rockets to 85 points, a low for Houston in these playoffs. They\u2019ve taken two of the three games easily, and in the one they didn\u2019t, Curry wasn\u2019t whole. Well, he seems to be now, and what will that say for the Rockets and the rest of this much-anticipated series that could go flatter than Nebraska? The Warriors had a strong hunch that Curry\u2019s shooting problems were only a phase, and if so, that was Houston\u2019s biggest worry. Until Game 3, the Warriors had plenty of Kevin Durant but not much of anything else. And there was another Curry-fueled issue: The Rockets were purposely running switches that would put him on Harden, the matchup Houston wanted to see and exploit. The Rockets opened the game by constantly pulling these stunts and getting baskets. Kerr called timeout after one switch, where Harden left Curry on an island, and spoke with his player before the huddle. The Warriors made amends for that by having Curry fight through the screens and staying on his man. Yet another problem persisted: Curry missed six of his seven 3-pointers in the first half. \u201cI had the right intentions,\u201d he said. \u201cI had five wide open 3s and only one went in. I knew I had to keep searching and find some openings.\u201d So he kept shooting, no matter the circumstances. It was akin to a camper in the woods furiously trying to light an inferno by rubbing a pair of twigs together. The smoke appeared, then the flame, and the ball finally felt familiar to Curry once again. \u201cI always say you should never lose confidence,\u201d he said. He turned to a common formula that shooters use to find their touch: Go for layups and then short jumpers to regain confidence and keep the defense honest. Then, let it fly from deep. \u201cYou can\u2019t be that great of a scorer without mental toughness,\u201d Draymond Green said, \u201cbecause you\u2019re always going to face obstacles when it comes to shooting. He always has that `next shot\u2019 mentality. Kudos to him. That\u2019s something that\u2019s been the key to his career, his mental toughness. He wouldn\u2019t be who he is today.\u201d What was particularly pleasing for Curry was returning serve to Harden. After repeatedly being victimized by the Rockets\u2019 leading scorer on the other end of the floor, Curry suddenly chopped up Harden. He was isolated on Harden, an average defender at best, and the results were predictable. Curry made 10 of 12 shots in the second half and his 26 points came in only 16 minutes. From that point, it was routine for Curry once again: The shooting, the flow of the game and the energy from the crowd. The fans simply wouldn\u2019t let him drift back into that other world. Everyone had a hunch that it was only a matter of time for Curry, but still. And what does one do when normalcy makes a refreshing return? He breathes easily and lives freely, with emotion, with a snow angel on the court once he landed after a made basket, and of course, the curse word heard \u2018round the arena (and among lip readers around the world). To that, he shrugs. \u201cA lot of it was just talking to myself,\u201d he said. \u201cYou\u2019ve got to be your biggest fan sometimes. You\u2019ve got to find whatever to get yourself going. I mean obviously it felt good and you want to use that energy to show your teammates that you are here, you\u2019re with them, and get the crowd into it. \u201cYou come into transition and you find an open look and there\u2019s a collective hush in the crowd, especially in this building. It\u2019s a cool moment.\u201d Veteran NBA writer Shaun Powell has worked for newspapers and other publications for more than 25 years. Asked on Sunday if he\u2019s thinking about a lineup change, Celtics coach Brad Stevens played coy. \u201cI think you do that every day,\u201d he said. But you can be sure that Stevens has thought about it much more than usual in the wake of his team\u2019s 30-point loss in Game 3. And you can be almost as sure that Aron Baynes will be in the Celtics starting lineup for Monday\u2019s Game 4 (8:30 p.m. ET, ESPN). Stevens has been willing to change his starting lineup all season. When the Celtics won 16 straight games in October and November, Baynes was in and out of the lineup, exchanging spots with Marcus Morris. Seemingly, the Baynes-Morris decision was based on matchups, with Baynes in the lineup against bigger, less mobile frontlines. But Stevens doesn\u2019t put it like that. \u201cIt\u2019s all about trying to figure out if you can be successful offensively and defensively,\u201d Stevens said of lineup decisions last week. \u201cSometimes you feel like you can be a little bit more successful on one end than the other, but both ends have to be taken into account.\u201d Through the first two rounds, the Cleveland Cavaliers had been more successful, especially offensively, playing small, with Kevin Love at center and, essentially, four shooters around LeBron James. Their most-used lineup \u2013 George Hill, J.R. Smith, Kyle Korver, James and Love \u2013 had scored 126 points per 100 possessions in its 110 minutes together . The Cavs started that way in Game 1 and the Celtics opened with Morris instead of Baynes (who started all five games of the conference semifinals) to match up. Advantage: Boston. The Celtics led 20-7 before any substitutions were made in Game 1 , which they won by 25. In Game 2 , Tristan Thompson was in and Korver was out. The Cavs still lost, but the start wasn\u2019t so bad and the numbers when they played big vs. small were pretty good. Through two games, the Cavs had been outscored by 38 points and were a minus-7 in 29 minutes with a frontline of James, Love and Thompson . But Cleveland had outscored Boston, 31-18, in 12 minutes with James, Love and Thompson on the floor against Boston\u2019s small frontline of Morris and Al Horford. Just 12 minutes is a small sample size, but small sample sizes are all you get in a playoff series. And with the big vs. small success in mind, the Cavs started the same group in Game 3 . Having won the first two games, the Celtics didn\u2019t make any changes either. Game 3 was a blowout and, essentially, nothing worked for the Celtics. But the Cavs\u2019 big frontline was a problem again, particularly when the Celtics played small. Cleveland outscored Boston 22-8 in just nine minutes with James, Love, Thompson, Morris and Horford on the floor with no Celtics center (Baynes or Greg Monroe). In playing big vs. small, the Cavs have been good on both ends of the floor. Thompson has made himself useful on offense as a screen-setter, as a roll man and as an offensive rebounder. \u201cHe might not always score the basketball, but he\u2019s going to get us extra possessions,\u201d Love said of Thompson after Game 3. \u201cHe\u2019s going to make the hustle plays, and he\u2019s going to do a lot of stuff that might not necessarily show up in the box score.\u201d But the bigger difference compared to how the Cavs have played overall has been on defense, where Boston has scored just 26 points (on an effective field goal percentage of 33 percent) in the 21 minutes with James, Love, Thompson, Morris and Horford on the floor without a Boston center. Over 87 possessions with Thompson as his primary defender, Horford (the Celtics\u2019 most efficient scorer in the postseason) has taken just six shots. Does Baynes, averaging six points per game this season, fix that? The Celtics have been better offensively with Baynes on the floor this series , but his presence doesn\u2019t necessarily free Horford of Thompson, because the Cavs have been willing to defend Baynes with Love . And generally, Baynes\u2019 value comes on defense. In the regular season, Boston allowed just 97.0 points per 100 possessions with him on the floor. That was the lowest on-court DefRtg among players who averaged at least 15 minutes in 40 or more games. With Baynes and Horford on the floor together, the Celtics allowed just 95.0 points per 100 possessions , the lowest mark for any two-man combination in the league that played as many minutes as they did. Assuming Baynes is the starting center on Monday with Horford at power forward and Terry Rozier at point guard, Stevens has a decision to make with the other two players on the floor, some combination of Morris, Jaylen Brown and Jayson Tatum. In the regular season, Morris came off the bench when Baynes started. Tatum has started all 95 games he\u2019s played this season, while Brown has only come off the bench in Games 2-4 of the conference semifinals, when he was on a minutes limit after suffering a hamstring injury in Game 7 of the first round. The Celtics have played just 31 minutes (regular season and playoffs) with Baynes, Horford, Brown and Morris on the floor without Tatum, and just 70 minutes with Baynes, Horford, Morris and Tatum on the floor without Brown. But defending the Cavs begins with defending James, and that has been Morris\u2019 job. He has been the primary defender on James for 101 of a possible 217 possessions in this series, with Brown (35) or Tatum (6) defending James for just 41 possessions total. In each of the last four halves, with the Cavs playing big, Baynes has been the first sub for the Celtics (as early as two and a half minutes into the half). And he\u2019s always replaced Tatum (three times) or Brown, but not Morris. Maybe, because Celtics have been switching most screens in this series, the initial defender on James isn\u2019t as critical as it may seem. And the Brown-Tatum combination offers more offense, important given Baynes\u2019 limitations on that end of the floor. Of course, the Celtics don\u2019t switch with Baynes at center as much as they do with Horford at center. Stevens was asked what would go into deciding who else would be on the floor if, hypothetically, Baynes was in the starting lineup. Would it be determined by the James matchup, by Morris\u2019 comfort in coming off the bench or something else? \u201cThere are two sides of the floor,\u201d Stevens said, maybe downplaying the importance of who\u2019s defending the best player in the world or perhaps just being coy again. \u201cSo there\u2019s a lot that goes into it. We\u2019ll make that decision based on both sides.\u201d Stay tuned. John Schuhmann is a staff writer for NBA.com. You can e-mail him here , find his archive here and follow him on Twitter . He didn\u2019t take over, force the issue, put the Cleveland Cavaliers\u2019 on his broad back or otherwise go crazy. Rather than posting some stats line for the ages built from crooked numbers, overstuffed categories, and desperation, the Cavaliers star played masterfully against the Boston Celtics on Saturday night at Quicken Loans Arena, but in a more nuanced way. In a way he, too, definitely prefers. Oh James produced his share of marvelous moments in Cleveland\u2019s lopsided-from-the-start 116-86 victory in Game 3 of the Eastern Conference finals . But they were beautiful basketball plays as opposed to superhero exploits. With the Cavs staring into an 0-2 hole in the best-of-seven series as the night began, the likelihood of James having to answer his team\u2019s bat signal in full cowl-scowl-and-score mode seemed great. But when teammates such as George Hill showed newfound aggressiveness early, when erratic gunner J.R. Smith demonstrated his stroke, when big men Kevin Love, Tristan Thompson and Larry Nance Jr. stayed mobile and active, James could cruise in the gear that suits him best: initiating the Cleveland offense without necessarily finishing it. He\u2019s always been a passer first; Magic Johnson by way of Muscle Beach. On the nights things go best for the Cavs, that\u2019s the role in which he thrives. \u201cI think my passing is up there with every other aspect of my game,\u201d James said after scoring 27 points, making eight of his modest 12 field goal attempts while distributing 12 assists. \u201cIt was something I kind of just knew I had when I first started playing the game of basketball, to be able to see things develop before they actually developed. Then it was on me to kind of put the ball on time, on target to my teammates ever since I was a kid, and I started playing at age nine.\u201d Cavs coach Tyronn Lue preceded James in talking with reporters by at least 20 minutes and, yes, he more or less predicted what the four-time Kia MVP would say. \u201cOne thing about it \u2013 he prides himself on \u2018on time, on target,\u2019\u201d Lue said. \u201cAnd when guys are open, he wants to make sure they have it right in their shooting pocket to get their shot off. You could see that with [Kyle] Korver tonight. You could see it with J.R. Kevin had a few that he missed that he normally makes. But he really prides himself on passing on time, on target.\u201d James was asked afterward about several specific deliveries, including a pair of left-handed bounce passes out of double teams that found first Nance, then Thompson for easy baskets. Given that two men on the ball only seems to trigger James\u2019 resourcefulness and creativity in sending it elsewhere, it\u2019s on Celtics coach Brad Stevens now to find ways to snuff such satisfying (for the opposition) plays. \u201cThere\u2019s only one person that can make passes going to his left like that in the league,\u201d Stevens said. \u201cBut we\u2019ve got to do a better job with all of our help and with all of our coverages.\u201d Certain elements of Cleveland\u2019s bounce-back performance were expected. The Cavaliers were going to shoot better from 3-point range if for no other reason than they hardly could shoot worse. After going 14-of-57 in the first two games, they pelted the Celtics by hitting 17-of-34 . Hill was an obvious candidate to boost his results by picking up his pace. With the extra days between Games 2 and 3, Lue and others had implored him to play faster, if only to let the Cavs\u2019 offense get into second and third options ahead of the unforgiving shot clock. And Smith, well, there\u2019s little predictable about his shooting from one game to the next, but it\u2019s reasonable to assume Lue would have had a quick hook for him had he not found his groove. All those things coming together \u2013 against a young Boston team that, on the road in these playoffs, has been a shell of its usual self \u2013 had the Cavaliers humming as if Toronto was in town. Korver got nine of his 14 points in the inconsequential fourth quarter, but all five Cavs starters had reached double figures by the end of the third. There was one more reason James didn\u2019t have to overly extend himself with the ball in his hands: Cleveland perked up defensively against a team that had embarrassed it in the two Boston games. One noticeable adjustment was shifting James\u2019 focus onto Celtics wing Jaylen Brown while putting a little less emphasis on forward Jayson Tatum. Brown was the one who had hurt the Cavs most, particularly early, with 46 points combined in Games 1 and 2. This time, Brown shot 3-of-8 and scored only 10. \u201cWe know Jaylen is a first-quarter player,\u201d Lue said. \u201cHe plays good throughout the game, but he really sets the tone early in that first quarter. So we wanted to do a good job on him. I thought LeBron really did a good job of closing out to him, making him put it on the floor, cutting him off and making him play in the crowd.\u201d James staying engaged and energetic defensively \u2013 he blocked two shots and had a pair of steals \u2013 naturally rubbed off on his teammates. \u201cTremendous connectivity\u201d and \u201cgreater togetherness,\u201d Stevens called it, lauding Cleveland for how thorough and of a piece its game plan was. \u201c Just tried to put myself in position to help our team,\u201d James said. \u201cAs a group, even when things broke down, we just covered for one another. We made them make extra passes. We made them make extra dribbles. We were flying around, and I just happened to be one of the guys on the floor that wanted to fly around as well.\u201d James was done flying around about five minutes into the final quarter. The other Cleveland starters didn\u2019t play at all in that period, and the garbage time offered some low-risk time to Jordan Clarkson, Jeff Green and Nance. James was left, at one point, to play peek-a-boo with his young daughter in the stands at the Q. Which had to be a lot more fun than playing peek-a-boo with whichever Cavaliers teammates were or weren\u2019t going to show up. * * * Steve Aschburner has written about the NBA since 1980. And the action on the floor isn\u2019t too bad either for a series that\u2019s all tied up. Yes, away from the game, these two are now talking a good game. The spice level escalated a bit when Rockets coach Mike D\u2019Antoni said Houston \u201ccan beat anybody, anywhere, anytime\u201d just before the Western Conference finals shifted to Oakland. He presumably means the Rockets can own either of the next two games, or both, at Oracle Arena starting Sunday ( Game 3 on TNT, 8 ET ), where visiting teams are known to suffer badly. And why shouldn\u2019t he feel frisky about that? The Rockets were road-tested all season and into the playoffs and did kick off 2017-18 by spoiling championship ring night for the Warriors here back in October in a distant prelude to the conference finals. Just as well, the Warriors believe their Game 2 issues , mainly a lost touch from deep by Steph Curry and shoddy defense, were nothing but lapses and that a return to normalcy is on tap, even against the team with the NBA\u2019s best record who ran them off the floor three days ago. What say you, Klay Thompson? \u201cI\u2019ll be better. Guarantee it.\u201d And Steve Kerr? \u201cWe\u2019re very confident going into (today).\u201d Surely Draymond Green of all people backed away from saying anything bold, right? \u201cWe\u2019re at our best when we feel threatened. And we feel threatened.\u201d Both teams agree on this much, that the series is about to get tighter and perhaps better because both teams bring out so much in the other. In the process, the usual playoff mainstays, such as home court advantage and adjustments and carryover from one game to the next, could be deemed non-issues. Anything goes, anything\u2019s possible. That said, the Warriors don\u2019t believe the series is in danger of slipping away from them, and if anything feel very much in control because, they say, their imperfections are fixable. The two most glaring ones, Curry\u2019s shooting and defense, are tried and true. How often does Curry go more than a few games with a faulty jumper? And the next time the Warriors\u2019 defense goes a long stretch with constant breakdowns in games of importance will be the first in this era. Perhaps the biggest factor in the Warriors\u2019 favor is they recognize how fatal these imperfections will be if they continue against a team as stacked as the Rockets. They need Curry\u2019s points to serve as a 1-2 punch with Kevin Durant. They need to be at their defensive finest because Houston is bringing James Harden and Chris Paul and also Eric Gordon on fire off the bench. The Warriors\u2019 confidence is not only natural considering they\u2019ve won two titles in three years, but it\u2019s also necessary in this series, which could ultimately decide the next NBA champion. \u201cIt\u2019s time to lock in for the remainder of the series,\u201d said Green. As the Warriors spoke Saturday during practice, Curry put in extra work. He has only a pair of three-pointers in two games, or three fewer than PJ Tucker. Even more, the Rockets are aggressively attacking him defensively, forcing him into switches on the pick and roll that place him in the direct path of Harden in numerous cases, a clear Houston advantage. By forcing him to labor on both ends, the Rockets feel he\u2019ll be one less worry for them, or at least someone who won\u2019t impact the game heavily. So far, he hasn\u2019t. Curry says his knee injury from three months ago is fine, although he\u2019s about to play only his seventh playoff game. If he has no physical limitations as he says, then the Warriors hope it\u2019s just a matter of working off the rust from inactivity. \u201cHe doesn\u2019t need us to create looks for him,\u201d said Green. \u201cHe\u2019s going to do that himself, and we know he will.\u201d It\u2019s all intertwined, the offensive link between Curry, Thompson and Durant. Against all but a handful of teams, the Warriors can easily overcome one of them misfiring or even two. But they\u2019re playing the Rockets, a historical offensive team, and scoring is at a premium if only to keep up. Durant has yet to see any slippage, but Thompson and Curry flopped in Game 2 and the outcome, therefore, was predictable. \u201cI think we\u2019ll be fine,\u201d said Thompson. \u201cAnd once Steph gets a couple of open looks and gets going, he can make any shot in the book.\u201d The bigger worry of the two, the Warriors say, is defense only because the Rockets test those limits. Game 2 showed the damage the Rockets are capable of inflicting if role players take advantage of Harden\u2019s isolation game and hit their shots. Gordon outscored the Warriors\u2019 starting backcourt by himself in Game 2 and is making over half his shots both beyond and within the three-point stripe. He was joined in Game 2 by Tucker and Trevor Ariza piling up points on the confused and suddenly-overmatched Warriors. \u201cWhen you get 22 points from a guy (Tucker) you don\u2019t plan on getting it from, then yeah, (defense) needs to be better,\u201d said Green. Both teams know each other and also reflect each other on offense, with plenty of isolation and deep shooting. The Warriors have rings the Rockets crave yet Houston is built on veterans with Paul and Harden capable of being aces in the clutch. Then it comes down to which team shows fewer bad stretches and plays with more urgency. Well: Golden State, which hasn\u2019t trailed 2-1 in a series since Oklahoma City in 2016 \u2014 which they eventually rallied and won \u2014 is pushing the red alert in a series that\u2019s tied and will be played on their court the next two games. \u201cWhen we\u2019re threatened, we play with great urgency and hustle,\u201d said Thompson. \u201cAlong with our talent, that\u2019s a great combination right there. It tends to bring out the best in this team. \u201cBeing threatened is a good feeling for us.\u201d Veteran NBA writer Shaun Powell has worked for newspapers and other publications for more than 25 years. He comes by that elusive trait naturally. Browns parents were college athletes. Both played sports at Texas A&M-Kingsville, where they met. Troy Brown, Sr. was an undersized power forward. \u201cHe wasn\u2019t gifted with height,\u201d Brown, Jr. says. \u201cTo be an undersized four-man, he had to work harder than most guys. He wanted to go out and get every rebound, and to do that, he had to outwork his opponents. He taught me the value of hard work, of having a work ethic. He taught me to stay in the gym and be a gym rat.\u201d The 6-foot-7 Brown\u2019s mother Lynn ran track and played volleyball. From her, Brown says, he inherited his athletic ability. Here\u2019s something else that\u2019s important to note about Brown\u2019s parents. They\u2019re both corrections officers in Nevada. It came as no surprise that the Brown\u2019s children were athletes, and good ones. Oldest daughter Janae won the shot put at the 2002 National Junior Olympics. She was 12 at the time. Brown\u2019s other sister Jada, also older than him, won a Nevada high school basketball championship at Centennial High School and was also chosen the state\u2019s player of the year in 2011. She played four years at Kansas. \u201cThat was a very competitive environment to grow up in,\u201d Brown says. \u201cMy sisters did their fair share of picking on me when I was younger. But they also took me under their wings. I watched them grow and I saw their successes, and their mistakes. They gave me inspiration.\u201d Brown tinkered with soccer and track, but there was never any doubt he was going to play basketball. His father didn\u2019t push him into the game, but he provided helpful tools along the way. At three, Brown, Jr. was already dunking on a toy plastic rim, jumping off furniture to heighten the degree of difficulty and foreshadowing a day when he would throw down a vicious slam over future Duke star and lottery pick Marvin Bagley. Once Brown, Jr. decided basketball was his game, he dropped all other sports and committed himself. Obviously, his father also gave him an appreciation for great players of the past. Young Brown became a film junkie, watching everything he could get his hands on. Larry Bird and Penny Hardaway were two favorites. Brown studied their every move. \u201cThe thing that stood out about those guys was whether they were making plays for teammates or creating for themselves, they were always playing downhill,\u201d Brown says. \u201cLarry Bird wasn\u2019t the most athletic player, but he knew how to use his body to finish at the rim, and he had a lot of counter moves in his repertoire.\u201d Brown chose his role models well, and like his sisters, developed aptitude for his sport at an early age. He began playing competitive basketball at four. When he was in second grade, his AAU team competed in the national championship tournament. By the time he was in eighth grade, he was the scourge of Las Vegas youth basketball. Brown could have played at one of two Las Vegas powerhouse prep schools \u2014 Bishop Gorman or Findlay Prep \u2014 but he chose to take the road less travelled, like his sisters, and attend a public school, Centennial. By 14, he\u2019d received his first Division I scholarship offer, from hometown UNLV. By the time he was a senior, he was a five-star recruit, and a McDonald\u2019s All-American. Brown started every game of his high school career and helped lead Centennial to a 95-17 record. Recruiting analysts labeled him a small forward, but Brown was a playmaker. When Brown was as sophomore, Duke coach Mike Krzyzewski came to see him play. He saw a future Magic Johnson. \u201cIt was a compliment I\u2019ll never forget,\u201d Brown says, \u201cand I truly believed it. I\u2019ve worked every day to try to live up to that comparison.\u201d Brown could have played at any school he wanted. He picked Oregon and coach Dana Altman, where during his only season, he learned an important lesson that he\u2019ll carry with him to the NBA. \u201cKYP,\u201d Brown says. \u201cKnow your personnel. Learning guys\u2019 strong suits and what they can do well. Put them in position to do what they do well, especially with the ball in my hands.\u201d Brown had a good, not great freshman season, during which he stuffed his stat sheet on a regular basis. He led the Ducks in rebounding, steals and free-throw attempts while averaging 11.3 points. Oregon had Payton Pritchard to play the point, but Brown was a playmaker too. \u201cI would definitely say I\u2019m a playmaker first, a scorer second,\u201d he says. \u201cSometimes that can be a weakness. I try to make plays for people when they aren\u2019t there. I should learn to attack more if a pass isn\u2019t there. But I\u2019ve always gotten more joy out of seeing my teammates get the basketball and score.\u201d When Brown decided to declare for the Draft, Altman summed up the state of his game. \u201cHe does have to improve his shooting,\u201d Altman said. \u201cI think that would be what most NBA teams would want him to do. His ball skills are very good and his instincts are very good, and now it\u2019s more matching that with the personnel he\u2019s with. I\u2019d like to see his assist-to-turnover ratio get a little better, which it would. \u2026 He\u2019s only 18, so his body will continue to develop and get stronger. There\u2019s a lot of upside. He\u2019s going to make big strides.\u201d Brown has been putting in some serious work at Impact Basketball in Las Vegas, working on his jump shot \u2014 he shot 29.1 percent from 3-point range at Oregon \u2014 and his ball handling. His decision making will improve with age and experience. If Brown does shore up his perimeter game, he\u2019ll become a prototypical modern-day NBA player. Position-less basketball has become the norm, and Brown has the requisite size and most of the skills needed to plug into the right system. \u201cThat\u2019s very important to me,\u201d Brown says. \u201cGoing to the right system. You want to be picked as high as possible, but at the end of the day, if you don\u2019t go into a system that fits you, you might be a high Draft pick and be out of the league in a couple of years.\u201d * * * Chris Dortch is the editor of the Blue Ribbon College Basketball Yearbook . The views on this page do not necessarily reflect the views of the NBA, its clubs or Turner Broadcasting."},{"id":46864,"link":"https:\/\/www.nba.com\/news\/boston-celtics-cleveland-cavaliers-key-numbers-after-game-1-and-2-series","date":"2018-05-18","title":"Numbers breakdown: Why Boston Celtics have 2-0 lead in Eastern Conference finals","content":" Are we witnessing the end of LeBron James\u2019 dominance of the Eastern Conference? For the first time in the last eight years, James\u2019 team trails an East playoff series by two games. And the team that\u2019s up 2-0 in the Eastern Conference finals is the one missing two of its three highest-paid players and one that struggled to get through the first round almost as much as the Cleveland Cavaliers did. Forget what we\u2019ve seen from Cleveland all season; If you watched the Boston Celtics through six games against the Milwaukee Bucks, you\u2019d have a hard time believing that they\u2019d go on to win seven of their next eight playoff games, with the postseason\u2019s best NetRtg (point differential per 100 possessions) over that stretch . So how did we get here? Here are some numbers to know about the Celtics and Cavs, with the series taking three days off before it resumes with Game 3 in Cleveland on Saturday ( 8:30 p.m. ET, ESPN ). The Celtics ranked 18th offensively in the regular season , slightly worse than the league average in effective field goal percentage, offensive rebounding percentage and free throw rate (and right at the league average in turnover rate). They ranked third in effective field goal percentage from outside the paint, but 28th in field goal percentage in the paint. In 22 games without Kyrie Irving, they scored just 102.6 points per 100 possessions (a rate which would have ranked 25th).  In the playoffs, the Celtics have been more efficient offensively ( 107.7 points scored per 100 possessions ) than the Cavs (107.4). Boston is one of only two playoff teams (Indiana was the other) that have scored more efficiently in the postseason than they did in the regular season. Through the first two rounds, the Celtics\u2019 effective field goal percentage took a dip, but they cut down on turnovers and saw an increase in free throw rate. In the conference finals, they\u2019ve been able to get to the basket more often, with 38 percent of their shots coming in the restricted area, up from 32 percent over the first two rounds. And that goes to the fact that they\u2019re playing a bad defensive team; The Cavs ranked 29th defensively this season . In the previous 40 years, no team that had ranked in the bottom thee defensively in the regular season had won a playoff series. So the Cavs have already made history, but it\u2019s not a coincidence that the only other team that scored more points per 100 possessions in the playoffs than they did in the regular season is the team \u2014 the Indiana Pacers \u2014 that the Cavs played in the first round. Even in getting swept by the Cavs in the conference semifinals, the Toronto Raptors were just as efficient offensively ( 110.1 points scored per 100 possessions ) as they were in the first round ( 110.2 ). But the Celtics had an improved offense even before they got to this series. They\u2019ve been incredibly balanced, with six guys in their rotation having a postseason usage rate between 19.6 and 24.0 (none higher than 28th overall in these playoffs ). As Al Horford said after Game 1, they\u2019ve found \u201cwhat fits this group.\u201d They\u2019ve also found, against the Philadelphia 76ers and Cavs in particular, matchups that can be taken advantage of. They\u2019ve been able to execute with precision and patience. Two things stand out about the Cleveland offense as it has scored less than a point per possession in each of the first two games . First, LeBron James has just 18 total points in the restricted area after averaging a postseason-high 14.4 points per game in the restricted area through the first two rounds . The Celtics have done well in transition (the Cavs have just 16 total fast break points) and in staying in front of James in half-court situations. Even when he scored 21 points in the first quarter of Game 2, five of his eight buckets came from outside the paint . And over the two games, only 27 percent of his shots have come from the restricted area, down from 41 percent through the first two rounds. James has shot 77 percent in the restricted area and 29 percent from 3-point range in the playoffs, so if most of his offense continues to come from the perimeter, the Celtics are in great shape.  The other thing that stands out (regarding the Cleveland offense) from Games 1 and 2 is that the Cavs have shot just 1-for-10 on corner 3-pointers . Game 1 was just the fourth game this season in which the Cavs didn\u2019t make a corner three, and the two games are just the third time in the four seasons since James returned to Cleveland that they\u2019ve made fewer than two corner threes over a two-game stretch. Corner threes are one thing that distinguishes the Toronto series from the other two that the Cavs have played. They averaged 5.3 per game against the Raptors, but just 2.3 against the Pacers and now, 0.5 against the Celtics. It\u2019s about the attempts as much as it is about the success rate. In the regular season, Boston opponents took only 19 percent of their 3-pointers from the corners. That was the lowest opponent rate in the league. The lack of corner threes is tied to James\u2019 lack of shots in the restricted area. Because the Celtics have done a good enough job of staying in front of James, they\u2019ve been able to (generally) stay at home on the Cavs\u2019 shooters. If the Cavs can find a way to get their star to the basket, the Boston defense will have to react and other things will open up. The Cavs started Tristan Thompson in Game 2, with the thought that he had matchup advantage (on the glass, in particular) against Horford. In last year\u2019s conference finals, the Cavs outscored the Celtics by 81 points in 134 minutes with both Thompson and Horford on the floor . In this series, the Cavs are a plus-4 in 18 minutes with Thompson on the floor and Horford playing center, and that probably keeps Thompson in the starting lineup for Game 3. Having been outscored by 38 points overall in the series, Cleveland should see some encouragement in any configuration that has produced a positive point differential. Before Game 2, Celtics coach Brad Stevens said he wouldn\u2019t change his starting lineup if the Cavs did. But the Celtics didn\u2019t let the Horford-Thompson matchup last too long, especially in the second half, when Aron Baynes subbed in for Jayson Tatum less than 2 1\/2 minutes into the third quarter. Thompson has played more minutes (20) in the series with Horford at power forward (alongside Baynes or Greg Monroe ) than with Horford at center (18). And the Celtics have outscored the Cavs by nine points in those 20 minutes with Thompson on the floor and Horford at the four. With their wins in Games 1 and 2, the Celtics are 9-0 at home in the postseason . If they remain undefeated at TD Garden, they\u2019ll win this series. But the Celtics have been more than 20 points per 100 possessions better at home (plus-10.8) than on the road (minus-9.4) in the postseason. Their only road win (Game 3 at Philadelphia) came in overtime and the only team with a bigger home-road NetRtg differential (26.5) in these playoffs is the team ( Milwaukee ) that the Celtics played in the first round. The Cavs are 5-1 at home and have been 11.7 points per 100 possessions better at Quicken Loans Arena than they\u2019ve been on the road in the playoffs. But four of those five home wins have been by four points or less . They haven\u2019t exactly taken care of business at The Q. * * * John Schuhmann is a staff writer for NBA.com. In this case: Save Our Season. \u201cI can\u2019t do it by myself,\u201d Harden said. Harden pumped 41 points yet came up empty, in terms of the Game 1 score and the quest to keep home-court advantage against the Warriors. Aside from an additional lift from Chris Paul, the Rockets offered little else and so Harden felt compelled to grab a bullhorn and let it be known: This isn\u2019t how we got here. Evidently the Rockets listened to their finest player, their gut feeling and the drumbeat of desperation and decided to do something about it. And so the Western Conference finals , along with the Rockets, are far from done. There\u2019s more to the Rockets, meaning, beyond what they opened the series with. It\u2019s all even at one game each because Eric Gordon, P.J. Tucker and Trevor Ariza finally lent a hand, a foot, a well-placed elbow and especially a jump shot to the cause. The Rockets felt normal in Wednesday\u2019s 127-105 wipeout win because they felt whole. They took the lead almost from the jump, withstood a few teases from the Warriors in the second half and cruised. They looked more like the well-balanced Golden State Warriors than they did themselves. \u201cThey knew what they had to do and they did it,\u201d said Warriors guard Stephen Curry. Harden and Paul took most of the shots but the others made most of the plays that caused the beat-down. Ariza attacking and slashing? Done. Tucker dropping 3s from the corner? Yes. Gordon going iso from the key and hitting from 25 feet? Absolutely. These three are players who chip in and help out for the Rockets, usually yielding to Harden and Paul \u2014 or at least orbiting around them, looking for their opening. But Wednesday was a bit of a role switch. At times, Ariza and Gordon and Tucker took turns taking over the game and pushed the lead far beyond the Warriors\u2019 reach. Tucker and Ariza were a combined 3-for-11 shooting in 58 minutes in Game 1. From a functional standpoint, they were no more than hood ornaments. But they joined Gordon and were straight fire Wednesday. They combined for 68 points (compared to 43 for Harden-Paul) and more importantly were efficient with their aim (Ariza, 7-for-9 shooting; Tucker, 8-for-9 shooting; Gordon, 8-for-15). Even better for Houston? Harden and Paul looked for them constantly and fed them the ball. That\u2019s called respect. \u201cThey got exactly what they wanted tonight,\u201d said Warriors coach Steve Kerr. \u201cThey got everybody going. I mean, Ariza, Tucker, Gordon. They got everybody involved. The exact opposite of Game 1. They brought it. They got it done.\u201d The 22 points represent a career playoff-high for Tucker, and almost matched the combined total of Curry and Klay Thompson combined, and this came with a bit of irony as well. Tucker was signed as a free agent last summer precisely for this reason: to give Houston an edge at both ends in a possible Western Conference playoffs showdown against the Warriors. Tucker, however, wasn\u2019t the Rockets\u2019 first choice. That would be Andre Iguodala , and somewhat understandably so. Signing Iguodala would have stripped the Warriors of a veteran with strong postseason credentials. Imagine if Iguodala left the Warriors, joined Houston and helped eliminate his old team? \u201cWe tried,\u201d D\u2019Antoni said. \u201cIt was a no-brainer to try. I don\u2019t know how close we were. I knew it would be hard.\u201d Instead, Iguodala stayed put and the Rockets\u2019 Plan B was Tucker, a rugged forward who brought a reliable 3-point stroke from the corners and a no-nonsense approach. So it was with some added satisfaction that Tucker has at least shown up in this series while Iguodala is, for now, living off his reputation. Iguodala had five points and three turnovers Wednesday and, for the second straight game, didn\u2019t leave any meaningful impression. It\u2019s the second time this season where Tucker hurt the Warriors. The first came in the season-opener, when he went for 22 points and some clutch free throws in Houston\u2019s win in Oakland . \u201cThe guy\u2019s a warrior,\u201d said D\u2019Antoni. \u201cHe finds a way to affect the game.\u201d Gordon had a Splash Brothers-like game with six 3-pointers and 27 points (matching Harden) in 32 minutes, which came a few hours after he was announced as a finalist for the Kia Sixth Man award . \u201cI just wanted to be aggressive,\u201d Gordon said. \u201cIt\u2019s always good to be on a good team which makes my job easier. It\u2019s been a blast since I\u2019ve been here.\u201d Ariza was more economical than usual from deep, taking only three 3-pointers. Instead, he relied on his dribble game, which perhaps caught the Warriors by surprise. Scoring in transition and in the half court, Ariza finished plays and forced the Warriors\u2019 defense to pay close attention instead of doubling Harden. After major foul trouble in Game 1, he avoided that and got his game on track. \u201cI forgot all about Game 1,\u201d Ariza said. \u201cI was excited to be on the court. I focused all of my thoughts into this game and what I could do to help the team.\u201d Was this a fleeting sighting for the Rockets that\u2019ll either fade or disappear completely when the series shifts to Oakland? D\u2019Antoni doesn\u2019t think so. He\u2019s leaning on a season\u2019s worth of evidence that tells him a 65-win team is more liable to reflect Game 2 than Game 1. For the Rockets\u2019 playoff health, that\u2019d better be true. \u201cYou\u2019re not going to come in and change the way you play,\u201d D\u2019Antoni said. \u201cWe are who we are, and we had to be who we are. We just did it better and for longer (tonight). We\u2019re very comfortable about who we are. We can beat anybody, anywhere and anytime playing like the way we play. It\u2019s effective. It\u2019s efficient.\u201d For the next three off-days it\u2019s the Warriors who must solve mysteries for a change for their home games in Oakland. Curry was chilly in Game 2, raising questions about his recovery from his MCL injury and\/or his ability to shake free of Houston\u2019s defense. Meanwhile, Draymond Green\u2019s biggest contribution to the series so far was his Game 1 shove of Harden . \u201cWe don\u2019t really worry about who we\u2019re playing against,\u201d Tucker said. \u201cIf we come out and be some dogs and do what we did tonight, it doesn\u2019t matter. But if we don\u2019t, then we see the results in Game 1. So it\u2019s not about chess match or what they\u2019re doing. "},{"id":40480,"link":"https:\/\/www.nba.com\/news\/stephen-curry-struggles-mightily-golden-state-warriors-drop-game-2","date":"2018-05-17","title":"Stephen Curry struggles mightily as Golden State Warriors drop Game 2","content":"HOUSTON  \u2014 Stephen Curry\u2019s name is often the first one mentioned where the Golden State Warriors are concerned, even on a team with three other All-Stars and a fellow former Kia MVP winner in Kevin Durant. And there\u2019s good reason for it. Curry is the player most teams fear because of the way he changes the game\u2019s dynamics with his shooting range that knows no bounds. But he\u2019s also the one player teams can attack on the defensive end, marking perhaps the only real soft spot for the reigning NBA champions. Game 2 of the Western Conference finals provided a masterclass in how to exploit Curry\u2019s defensive struggles as the Houston Rockets targeted him early, often and relentlessly in a 127-105 blowout at Toyota Center Wednesday to even the series at 1-1. Curry, the two-time Kia MVP, knows what opposing scouting reports say about him. He\u2019s used to wearing a bullseye on defense and accepts the challenge of battling against his reputation. When it became obvious that he was going to be the center of defensive attention for a Rockets team desperate to avoid their first back-to-back playoff losses, Curry knew exactly what was coming. \u201cSurprise, surprise,\u201d Curry said of the Rockets\u2019 concerted effort to go at him. The strategy worked to perfection, sapping any chance of the Warriors duplicating their Game 1 effort that saw them withstand an early blitz before taking control in the second half and winning by 13 points. Durant was surgical again, shredding the Rockets for a game-high 38 points . Curry, however, finished with 16 on 19 shots, including a 1-for-8 performance on 3-poiners. Durant and Curry were the only Warriors to reach double figures as the Rockets dismantled the fabled \u201cHamptons Five\u201d lineup. \u201cWe got outplayed the whole game,\u201d Warriors coach Steve Kerr said. \u201cWe\u2019re playing a team that won 65 games. They\u2019re damn good. We got it handed to us. You can look at it anyway you want and parcel it out into different shifts and rotations and all that stuff, but it didn\u2019t matter who we had out there tonight. We got beat.\u201d The Warriors got knocked off balance early by the Rockets\u2019 ramped up energy and physicality and, outside of Durant, never fully answered that challenge on either end of the floor. \u201cI think that was the difference,\u201d Curry said. \u201cWe were trying to be too cute with our exchanges and our switches and all that stuff instead of just manning up and playing one-on-one defense. Whether they score or not, as hard as we\u2019re working on the defensive end, they\u2019re working as hard on the offensive end to get shots up. \u201cThat was the difference between Game 1 and Game 2. They made a concerted effort to turn those one-on-one situations into a little bit more ball movement, and we were just a step slow, myself included.\u201d Through two games in this series, Curry is the only principle who has not played up to his own standard. Wednesday was his sixth game back in the lineup after missing the final 10 regular-season games and first six of the playoffs while recovering from a Grade 2 MCL sprain . That injury came on the heels of a six-game absence from a right ankle sprain in early March. Curry and Kerr dismissed the notion that there was any lingering injury issue involved with Curry\u2019s play. Kerr responded to a reporter\u2019s question with a joke that he\u2019d attribute \u201c13.7 percent\u201d of Curry\u2019s subpar performance to the knee sprain. \u201cI mean, it obviously wasn\u2019t Steph\u2019s best night,\u201d Kerr said. \u201cHe struggled. But it\u2019s just one of those nights. \u201cHe\u2019s feeling good. I don\u2019t know what this was, maybe his sixth or seventh game back. So he\u2019s getting better, and I thought he played a lot better in Game 1 than anyone gave him credit for. But, again, it wasn\u2019t our night. Our defense wasn\u2019t connected. We weren\u2019t on the same page on a lot of plays where we got a little out of sorts. Yeah, there are a lot of ways you could look at it, but it doesn\u2019t matter \u2014 127-105.\u201d Curry insists he\u2019s not dealing with any physical discomfort that would cause him to struggle the way he has so far in this series. \u201cI\u2019m feeling great,\u201d he said. \u201cTonight, I didn\u2019t find a rhythm early. I had some decent looks from 3 that could have changed the momentum of the game early in the first half. But for the most part it was just a frustrating night all the way around. They made some adjustments. They got other guys involved, and they made plays. \u201cThat\u2019s kind of how a series like this is going to be. Game after game, it\u2019s going to be a chess match. Tonight we just didn\u2019t make enough plays to stop the momentum in this building, and that was the difference in the game. P.J. Tucker, Trevor Ariza, Eric Gordon, they stepped up tonight. So we\u2019ve got to be ready for that kind of aggressiveness from them in Game 3 , and keep doing what we\u2019re doing.\u201d Actually, the Warriors need Curry to do something else. They have to find a way to get him back in a groove. Maybe heading back to Oracle Arena for Games 3 and 4 will provide the spark he needs. Following Durant\u2019s lead might be a good place to start. \u201cHe\u2019s been amazing,\u201d Curry said. \u201cHe\u2019s making plays. He\u2019s aggressive. I think we all need to feed off of that focus and that determination. I think our intentions were in the right place. Just the way that first quarter started, we really didn\u2019t deserve to be in the game down the stretch in the fourth quarter. \u201cObviously, he made a lot of tough shots and great plays to keep us close. If he can continue to do what he does, I\u2019ll shoot the ball better and play better defense, other guys will get involved and we\u2019ll be in great shape. Again, no panic. No house-is-on-fire type of mentality in our locker room. \u201cWe\u2019re in pretty good shape right now.\u201d * * * Sekou Smith is a veteran NBA reporter and NBA TV analyst. He took two dribbles and pulled up for a jumper from the right side of the floor with his toes on the 3-point line. It was, for many reasons, a terrible shot. It was a long 2-pointer, off the dribble, with 20 seconds left on the shot clock, and from one of the worst shooters in the league. There were 207 players who took at least 200 shots from outside the paint this season, and Marcus Smart (31 percent) ranked 199th among the 207 in field goal percentage on those shots. Through the Boston Celtics\u2019 first 13 playoff games, Smart had an effective field goal percentage of 28 percent on pull-up jumpers. He likely thought he was shooting a 3 in that situation, making it \u2014 maybe \u2014 not so terrible of a shot. But he was brutal 1-for-16 on pull-up 3s before Tuesday. And no, this one \u2014 no matter how many points it would have counted for \u2014 didn\u2019t go in either. But Smart didn\u2019t go to the bench because of it. Celtics coach Brad Stevens, a guy who cares about math, had been asked about Smart\u2019s shot selection less than three hours earlier. \u201cLet it fly,\u201d Stevens said. \u201cI want guys playing free. I want them excited to make a mistake and learn from it and move on. Marcus Smart makes a ton of winning plays, and because of that, he deserves to take those opportunities, because we all believe he\u2019s going to make them.\u201d Saying \u201cwe all believe\u201d that Smart is going to make his shots is a bit of a stretch. But Stevens proved prophetic. That wasn\u2019t the only bad shot Smart took as he went 3-for-9 on Tuesday. But he also made a ton of winning plays as the Celtics won Game 2, 107-94 , to take a 2-0 series lead. After a 21-point first quarter from LeBron James , the Cavs led by as many as 11 in the second, and by seven at halftime. But behind a big third quarter from Terry Rozier, the Celtics \u2014 who now have a league-high 18 wins after trailing by double-digits \u2014 came back. And they took the lead for good with a long, catch-and-shoot 3 from Smart with 4:34 to go in the period. Smart\u2019s last two buckets were much more characteristic of the hard-nosed player he is. Later in the third, he took a hand-off from Al Horford with Kyle Korver trailing the play. Smart drove into the paint, took off sideways to avoid the helping Kevin Love, and dropped in a reverse scoop shot as he fell to the ground. And midway through the fourth, after tapping out an offensive rebound, he outhustled James and Tristan Thompson for a tip-in of a Jayson Tatum miss, again falling to the ground as the shot dropped in. But of course, as is always the case with Smart, the 11 points he scored didn\u2019t tell nearly the whole story. There were the four steals, two of which sent Smart crashing out of bounds. And then, there was the playmaking. Stevens has plenty of options through which to run his late-game offense. It could be Horford in the post, Tatum from the left side of the floor, or a pick-and-roll with Rozier. For most of Tuesday\u2019s fourth quarter, the ball was in Smart\u2019s hands, running pick and roll with Greg Monroe and Horford. The results were mostly good, and Smart finished with nine assists and zero turnovers. As has been the case all postseason with the Celtics, they found an advantage and attacked it. \u201cAt one time they had Love on Smart, and that\u2019s just unique matchup for a big (to have to defend a pick-and-roll ballhandler),\u201d Stevens said. \u201cWe tried to get out of the screens quick and tried to find a seam to the rim, either on the drive or the lob.\u201d The shots from the outside don\u2019t matter as much if you\u2019re getting to the basket. And for the second straight game, the Celtics outscored the Cavs in the paint. This time the tally was 50-42, with six of Smart\u2019s nine assists resulting in layups or dunks for his teammates. \u201cHe has a really uncanny ability to get into the lane and either create for himself or create for others,\u201d James said. \u201cAnd he did that tonight with the nine assists, and also had some offensive rebounds and also some missed shots.\u201d Yes, the missed shots. It\u2019s part of the package. You take the bad with the good. In this series, the Celtics have been both the better team and the tougher team, and Smart, still playing with a splint on his right thumb, exemplifies the latter. \u201cThat\u2019s me,\u201d he said. \u201cI\u2019m the youngest of four boys.
